The Five Internal Senses and Their Functions
Definition and location of the five internal senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five internal senses, locate them in the brain cavities, and explain the argument distinguishing common sense from sight.

Core Idea
The inner senses are the architects of perception, turning fragmented sensations into coherent experience.

In this passage, the author introduces the five internal senses that process sensory data beyond the external senses. He particularly focuses on common sense, providing a vivid proof that it is distinct from sight using the example of a falling drop. We will dissect each faculty and the reasoning behind their localization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Internal Senses
The text lists five: الحس المشترك (common sense), الخيال (imagination), الوهم (estimation), الحافظة (memory), المتصرفة (compositive faculty). Note that some texts combine some of these.
2
Locate Common Sense and Imagination
Common sense is at the front of the first brain cavity; imagination at the back. The text uses lesions to prove this: front lesion impairs common sense, back lesion impairs imagination.
This shows empirical grounding in medieval Islamic psychology.
3
Understand the Proof for Common Sense
We see a falling drop as a straight line, but sight only sees the instantaneous drop. The continuous line must be perceived by another power inside the brain, which is common sense.
This reasoning distinguishes common sense from the external sense of sight.
4
Functions of Remaining Senses
Estimation perceives non-sensory meanings (e.g., enmity in a wolf); memory stores those meanings; the compositive faculty combines and separates images at will.
5
Apply the Lesion Test
If a patient has a lesion in the front of the first cavity, common sense is impaired; if in the back, imagination is impaired. This shows the physical basis of each power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التي في الباطن فهي أيضا خمس الحس المشترك والخيال والوهم والحافظة والمتصرفة. أما الحس المشترك فهي قوة مرتبة في مقدمة التجويف الأول في الدماغ تقبل جميع الصور المنطبعة في الحواس الظاهرة، وهي غير البصر؛ لأنا نشاهد القطرة النازلة خطا مستقيما، والنقطة الدائرة بسرعة خطا مستديرا، وليس ارتسامهما في البصر؛ إذ البصر لا يرتسم فيه إلا المقابل وهو القطرة والنقطة، فإذن ارتسامهما إنما يكون في قوة أخرى. التجويف: اعلم أن في الدماغ تجاويف ثلاثة وينقسم كل منها إلى مقدم ومؤخر، ويعرف تعيين المواضع للقوى بعروض الآفة فيها وبطلان فعلها، مثلا إذا لحقت الآفة في مقدم التجويف الأول اختل الحس المشترك، وإذا لحقت في آخره اختل الخيال، وقس على هذا. قوة أخرى ترتسم فيها صورة القطرة والنقطة إذا كانتا في موضع – وتبقى تلك الصورة فيها زمانا - وتحركتا بسرعة إلى موضع آخر، وارتسمت صورتهما حين كونهما في ذلك الموضع فاتصلت الصورة الأولى بالصورة الأخرى، وهكذا ارتسمت الصور -
As for those in the interior, they are also five: common sense, imagination, estimation, memory, and compositive faculty. As for common sense, it is a power situated at the front of the first cavity of the brain, which receives all forms impressed upon the external senses. It is not sight, for we observe the falling drop as a straight line, and the fast-circling point as a circular line. Their imprinting is not in sight, because only what is opposite [directly] is imprinted in sight, namely the drop and the point. So their imprinting must be in another power. The cavity: Know that in the brain there are three cavities, each divided into front and back. The assignment of places for the powers is known by the occurrence of lesions and the failure of their action. For example, if a lesion affects the front of the first cavity, common sense is impaired; if it affects the back, imagination is impaired. Etc. Another power in which the image of the drop and point are imprinted when they are in a place – and that image remains in it for a while – and they move quickly to another place, and their images are imprinted when they are in that place, so the first image connects with the other image, and thus the images are imprinted.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines the five internal senses: common sense (الحس المشترك), imagination (الخيال), estimation (الوهم), memory (الحافظة), and the compositive faculty (المتصرفة). The author focuses on common sense, describing its location in the front of the first brain cavity. It receives all forms from the external senses. A proof is given that common sense is distinct from sight: we perceive a falling drop as a straight line and a fast-circling point as a circular line, but the external eye only sees the drop and point as they are at an instant; the continuity of line must be perceived by an internal power. Then a note about brain cavities: there are three cavities, each with front and back. Lesions in specific parts indicate the location of each power (e.g., front of first cavity for common sense, back for imagination). The passage ends with a description of another power (likely the compositive faculty or imagination) that retains and connects images over time.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
الخيال
الوهم
الحافظة
المتصرفة

Checkpoint

A) Common sense is located in the heart

C) Common sense is a type of imagination
D) Common sense is not affected by lesions

Key Takeaway
The five internal senses are distinct faculties in the brain, each with a specific location and function, and their existence is proven by empirical evidence (lesions) and logical reasoning (falling drop example).

The Definition of Imagination (al-Khayal)
Author defines the imaginative faculty as a power preserving sensory images.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two functions of al-khayal as the faculty that preserves and represents sensory images, and explain its relationship to common sense.

Core Idea
Imagination is the treasury that stores and recalls what the senses gather.

In this passage, the author gives a precise definition of the faculty of imagination, locating it anatomically and describing its dual role. This definition is crucial for understanding how classical Islamic psychology explains memory and perception. The author also clarifies that imagination serves as the storehouse for the common sense, which synthesizes raw sensory data.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the Arabic text. What is this passage about?
The definiendum is الخيال (al-khayal), the imaginative faculty.
2
Extract the genus
What broader category does the author assign to al-khayal? Look for the word 'قوة' (power/faculty).
The genus is 'قوة' (quwwa), a power or faculty, which places it among the internal senses.
3
Find the differentia
What distinguishes al-khayal from other internal senses? Look for its specific functions: preserving and representing sensory images after the objects disappear.
The differentia is 'تحفظ صور المحسوسات وتمثلها بعد الغيبوبة' (preserves all forms of sensed objects and represents them after their disappearance).
4
Locate the anatomical specification
Where is al-khayal located according to the text? This is part of the full definition.
It is 'في مؤخر التجويف الأول' (in the posterior of the first cavity of the brain).
5
Clarify the relationship with common sense
How does the author describe the connection between al-khayal and al-hiss al-mushtarak?
Al-khayal is 'خزانة الحس المشترك' (the treasury of the common sense), meaning it stores the unified sensory impressions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الخيال فهو قوة مرتبة في مؤخر التجويف الأول، تحفظ جميع صور المحسوسات وتمثلها بعد الغيبوبة، وهي خزانة الحس المشترك.
As for imagination, it is a faculty located in the posterior of the first cavity, preserving all forms of sensed objects and representing them after their disappearance, and it is the treasury of the common sense.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the faculty of imagination (al-khayal) as one of the internal senses. The author gives a complete definition: it is a power (quwwa) situated in the posterior part of the first cerebral cavity. Its function is dual: it preserves (tahfazu) all images of perceived objects and represents them (tumaththiluha) after the objects have vanished from direct perception. Additionally, it is described as the treasury (khizanah) of the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak), meaning that it stores the unified sensory impressions from the five external senses. Note that the definition includes both the genus (power/faculty) and the differentia (preservation and representation). The location is specified anatomically, as was typical in classical Islamic psychology.

Key Terms
الخيال
الحس المشترك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It judges meanings like friend and foe.
B) It preserves and recollects sensory images.
C) It combines and separates images.
D) It drives movement.

Key Takeaway
Imagination (al-khayal) is a distinct internal sense that preserves and recalls sensory forms, acting as the treasury for the unifying common sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Estimative Faculty: Definition and Function
Author defines the estimative faculty as perceiving particular meanings in sensed objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of al-wahm and explain its function with an Islamic psychological example.

Core Idea
Wahm perceives particular meanings, not sensory data – it is the instinct that tells the sheep to flee the wolf.

In this passage, the author defines a key internal faculty: estimation (al-wahm). Unlike the five external senses, wahm does not perceive colors or sounds; it perceives particular non-sensory meanings attached to sensed objects. Understanding this faculty helps distinguish between sensory perception, imagination, and instinctive judgment – a critical distinction in Islamic psychology and epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum: 'al-wahm' (الوهم). Note that it is introduced with 'وأما' (as for), signaling a new definition.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'quwwa' (a faculty/power). It is located in the rear of the middle brain cavity. This places wahm among the internal faculties.
Later texts discuss wahm's location in the brain; this physical anchor is important for Islamic medicine.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is 'tudriku al-ma‘ani al-juz’iyya al-mawjuda fi al-mahsusat' (perceives particular meanings existing in sensed objects). This distinguishes it from senses (which perceive physical forms) and intellect (which perceives universals).
4
Understand the example
The example of the sheep judging the wolf as 'to be fled from' and its offspring as 'to be cherished' illustrates wahm's function. These are particular, non-sensory meanings (hostility, affection) that the sheep perceives instinctively.
Here 'al-quwwa al-hakima' (the judging faculty) is identified with wahm.
5
Confirm the definition type
This is a complete real definition (hadd haqiqi) because it gives both genus and differentia, plus a clarifying example. The definition includes all instances of wahm and excludes other faculties.
Memorize this structure: genus + differentia + example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوهم فهو قوة مرتبة في آخر التجويف الأوسط من الدماغ، تدرك المعاني الجزئية الموجودة في المحسوسات كالقوة الحاكمة في الشاة بأن الذئب مهروب عنه، والولد معطوف عليه.
As for estimation, it is a faculty located in the rear of the middle cavity of the brain, perceiving particular meanings existing in sensed objects, like the judging faculty in the sheep that the wolf is to be fled from, and the offspring is to be cherished.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the estimative faculty (al-wahm), also known as the estimation or instinct. It is a power (quwwa) located in the back of the middle brain cavity. Its function is to perceive particular meanings (ma‘ani juz’iyya) that are not themselves sensory but are attached to sensed objects. For example, a sheep instinctively perceives the meaning of hostility in the wolf and the meaning of affection in its own lamb. This is not a sensory perception but an immediate judgment. The definition includes the genus (power) and differentia (perception of particular non-sensory meanings), along with an illustrative example from animal behavior. Note that the example uses 'the judging faculty' (al-quwwa al-hakima) to describe the sheep's instinct, which is identified with wahm.

Key Terms
الوهم (al-wahm): Faculty perceiving particular non-sensory meanings in sensed objects.
المعاني الجزئية (al-ma‘ani al-juz’iyya): Particular meanings as opposed to universal concepts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Universal meanings.
B) Physical shapes and colors.
C) Particular non-sensory meanings in sensed objects.
D) The combination of images.

Key Takeaway
Wahm is an internal faculty that perceives particular non-sensory meanings attached to sensed objects, making it distinct from both external senses and rational intellect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proof of the Common Sense: The Argument from Continuous Motion
Author proves the existence of common sense by arguing that continuous perception requires a unifying faculty beyond the eye.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof for the existence of common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) using the example of a moving point and explain why the eye cannot unify successive images.

Core Idea
Continuous perception proves a unifying sense beyond the five external senses.

The author presents a simple but powerful observation: when you see a moving point, you perceive a continuous line, not a series of discrete dots. Yet the eye can only see what is directly opposite it at a single moment. How then do you experience continuity? The author argues this requires a faculty that gathers the successive images — the common sense. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the observation
Notice the example: a moving point is seen as a dot only when opposite the eye; when it departs, the vision in that spot ceases. The point reappears only in the next position.
2
Recognize the eye's limitation
The eye cannot hold multiple successive images simultaneously because it only registers the image at the moment of direct opposition. Once the point moves, that image is gone.
3
Infer the necessity of another faculty
Since we do perceive continuity (e.g., a line), the successive images must be gathered somewhere else. The eye cannot do it, so a different faculty must exist.
4
Name that faculty
This unifying faculty is called al-hiss al-mushtarak (common sense). It collects data from the five external senses and combines them into unified perceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأنت تعلم أن البصر لا ترتسم فيه إلا الصورة المقابلة له في موضع، وإذا زالت عن الموضع زالت المقابلة والرؤية في ذلك الموضع، فالمتعلقة في كل مكان إذا قوبلت بالبصر ترى نقطة، وبعد زوالها عن ذلك الموضع لا تبقى المقابلة، فلا ترى هناك، بل في الموضع الثاني، فتحقق أن اجتماع تلك الصورة المتعاقبة ليس في البصر، بل في قوة أخرى، وهي الحس المشترك.
And you know that nothing is impressed in the eye except the image opposite it in a place, and when it departs from the place, the opposition and vision in that place depart. So the continuously moving point, when it is opposite the eye, is seen as a dot, and after its departure from that place, opposition does not remain, so it is not seen there, but in the second place. So verify that the gathering of those successive images is not in the eye, but in another faculty, which is the common sense.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a proof (dalil) for the existence of the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). The author uses an observation: when we see a moving point (e.g., a falling drop of water), it appears as a continuous line, not as discrete dots. The argument is that the eye can only see an image at the moment it is directly opposite; once it moves, that image disappears. So the perception of a continuous line requires a faculty that combines the successive discrete impressions into a single continuous perception. Since the eye cannot do this, there must be another faculty—the common sense—which collects sensory data from the five senses and unifies them. This is a classic argument found in Aristotelian psychology. The author uses a physical example to demonstrate the need for an internal unifying sense.

Key Terms
الحس المشترك
الصورة المتعاقبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author conclude that the common sense exists?
A) Because the eye sees all images at once.
B) Because successive images cannot be gathered in the eye.
C) Because the ear also hears sounds.
D) Because the sheep flees from the wolf.

Key Takeaway
The perception of continuous motion requires a unifying faculty beyond the external senses, which is called common sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defining Memory (al-Hafiza) as the Treasury of Wahm
Definition of memory with anatomical and functional specification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to dissect the definition of al-hafiza into its genus and differentia and explain how it functions as the treasury of the estimative faculty.

Core Idea
Memory stores non-sensible meanings perceived by wahm—it is the treasury of instinctual knowledge.

The author presents a precise definition of memory, locating it anatomically in the brain and specifying its function. This definition is not just a description; it establishes memory's role in preserving the particular meanings that the estimative faculty (wahm) extracts from sensed objects. Understanding this definition is essential for seeing how the internal faculties work together in Islamic psychology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is 'al-hafiza' (memory). Note its Arabic definite article and its position in the sentence.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'quwwa' (faculty), further specified by its location: 'marattaba fi awwal al-tajwīf al-ākhar min al-dimāgh' (placed at the beginning of the other cavity of the brain).
This anatomical detail distinguishes it from other faculties.
3
Extract the differentia
The differentia is its function: 'tahfazu mā tudrikuhu al-quwwa al-wahmiyya min al-ma‘ānī al-juz’iyya ghayr al-mahsūsa' (preserves what the estimative faculty perceives of particular non-sensible meanings).
The meanings are non-sensible but attached to sensed objects.
4
Recognize the functional role
The definition ends by calling it 'khizānat al-quwwa al-wahmiyya' (the treasury of the estimative faculty). This shows that memory stores the outputs of wahm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحافظة: فهي قوة مرتبة في أول التجويف الآخر من الدماغ، تحفظ ما تدركه القوة الوهمية من المعاني الجزئية الغير المحسوسة الموجودة في المحسوسات، وهي خزانة القوة الوهمية. والدليل على ثبوتها أنا إذا أدركنا المعاني الجزئية من صور المحسوسات ثم ذهلنا عنها، ثم بعد زمان رأينا تلك الصورة نحكم أن تلك المعاني قد وجدناها فيها من قبل، فلو لم تكن تلك المعاني محفوظة فينا زمان الذهول لامتنع الحكم عليها أنها هي هي.
As for the memory, it is a faculty located in the beginning of the other cavity of the brain, preserving what the estimative faculty perceives of particular non-sensible meanings existing in sensed objects, and it is the treasury of the estimative faculty. The proof of its existence is that when we perceive particular meanings from the forms of sensed objects and then become heedless of them, then after a time we see that form and judge that those meanings we had found in it before—if those meanings were not preserved in us during the time of heedlessness, it would be impossible to judge that they are the same.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the faculty of memory (al-hafiza) and then provides a proof (dalil) for its existence. The definition states that memory is a power located in the anterior part of the third brain cavity. Its function is to preserve (tahfazu) the particular meanings (ma‘ani juz’iyya) that are perceived by the estimative faculty (al-wahm). These meanings are non-sensible (ghayr mahsusa) but are attached to sensed objects. Memory is thus the treasury (khizanah) of the estimative faculty, storing the instinctual meanings. The proof is an immediate inference: we often perceive a meaning (e.g., hostility in a wolf) and later, after a period of forgetfulness, we see the same object and recognize that we had perceived that meaning before. This recognition requires that the meaning was stored somewhere during the interval; otherwise, we could not judge it as the same. Therefore, memory must exist. This is a classic argument from the phenomenon of recognition. The proof is conditional: if recognition occurs, then there must be a storage faculty.

Key Terms
الحافظة
الوهمية
المعاني الجزئية
الذهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sensory forms (suwar) of objects
B) Particular non-sensible meanings perceived by wahm
C) Universal rational concepts
D) The material composition of the brain

Key Takeaway
Memory (al-hafiza) is the faculty that preserves the non-sensible particular meanings extracted by the estimative faculty (wahm), acting as its treasury.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Proving the Existence of Memory through Recognition
Proof of memory through recognition using a conditional argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument proving the existence of memory and identify its logical form as modus tollens.

Core Idea
Recognition proves memory: if we can judge that a meaning is the same as before, that meaning must have been stored.

After defining memory, the author provides a proof (dalil) for its existence. The proof is based on the common experience of recognition: we perceive a meaning (e.g., hostility in a wolf), forget it temporarily, and later upon seeing the same object, we recognize the meaning as the same. This recognition would be impossible unless the meaning was stored somewhere during the interval. Therefore, memory must exist. This is a logical argument that moves from an observable phenomenon to an underlying faculty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomenon
The author starts with our experience: we perceive particular meanings from sensed objects, then become heedless (dhahulna), then later see the same form and judge that we had found those meanings before.
2
State the conditional
The argument is: if the meanings were not preserved during the interval, then it would be impossible to judge them as the same. This is a conditional (if-then) statement.
Arabic: 'فلو لم تكن تلك المعاني محفوظة فينا زمان الذهول لامتنع الحكم عليها أنها هي هي'
3
Recognize the antecedent and consequent
Antecedent (al-muqaddam): 'the meanings were not preserved in us during the time of heedlessness'. Consequent (al-tali): 'it would be impossible to judge that they are the same'.
4
Apply modus tollens
Since we do judge them as the same (negation of consequent), it follows that the meanings were preserved (negation of antecedent). This is a valid inference called modus tollens.
Formally: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحافظة: فهي قوة مرتبة في أول التجويف الآخر من الدماغ، تحفظ ما تدركه القوة الوهمية من المعاني الجزئية الغير المحسوسة الموجودة في المحسوسات، وهي خزانة القوة الوهمية. والدليل على ثبوتها أنا إذا أدركنا المعاني الجزئية من صور المحسوسات ثم ذهلنا عنها، ثم بعد زمان رأينا تلك الصورة نحكم أن تلك المعاني قد وجدناها فيها من قبل، فلو لم تكن تلك المعاني محفوظة فينا زمان الذهول لامتنع الحكم عليها أنها هي هي.
As for the memory, it is a faculty located in the beginning of the other cavity of the brain, preserving what the estimative faculty perceives of particular non-sensible meanings existing in sensed objects, and it is the treasury of the estimative faculty. The proof of its existence is that when we perceive particular meanings from the forms of sensed objects and then become heedless of them, then after a time we see that form and judge that those meanings we had found in it before—if those meanings were not preserved in us during the time of heedlessness, it would be impossible to judge that they are the same.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage defines the faculty of memory (al-hafiza) and then provides a proof (dalil) for its existence. The definition states that memory is a power located in the anterior part of the third brain cavity. Its function is to preserve (tahfazu) the particular meanings (ma‘ani juz’iyya) that are perceived by the estimative faculty (al-wahm). These meanings are non-sensible (ghayr mahsusa) but are attached to sensed objects. Memory is thus the treasury (khizanah) of the estimative faculty, storing the instinctual meanings. The proof is an immediate inference: we often perceive a meaning (e.g., hostility in a wolf) and later, after a period of forgetfulness, we see the same object and recognize that we had perceived that meaning before. This recognition requires that the meaning was stored somewhere during the interval; otherwise, we could not judge it as the same. Therefore, memory must exist. This is a classic argument from the phenomenon of recognition. The proof is conditional: if recognition occurs, then there must be a storage faculty.

Key Terms
الحافظة
الذهول
الوهمية
المعاني الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What logical inference does the author use to prove memory?
A) Modus ponens: If P then Q; P; therefore Q
B) Modus tollens: If P then Q; not Q; therefore not P
C) Affirming the consequent
D) Denying the antecedent

Key Takeaway
The conditional argument from recognition proves that memory must exist to preserve meanings during periods of heedlessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the conditional statement in the proof and rewrite it in if-then form. Then determine whether the argument is valid by checking if the conclusion necessarily follows.
?

The Compositive Faculty and Its Two Modes
Author defines the compositive faculty, then explains it becomes mutakhayyila or mutafakkira depending on whether used by wahm or aql.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the mutakhayyila and mutafakkira modes of the compositive faculty and identify which higher faculty controls each.

Core Idea
The compositive faculty serves both animal instinct and human reason depending on who commands it.

In Islamic philosophical psychology, the internal senses are not static. This lesson explores a key faculty – the compositive imagination – that can operate under two different masters: estimation (wahm) or intellect (aql). Understanding this distinction deepens our model of the soul's faculties and their roles in perception and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the faculty being defined
Locate the term 'al-mutasharrafa' in the Arabic passage. Note that it is a power (quwwa) situated in the middle ventricle of the brain. Its function is to combine and separate the contents of imagination and memory.
This faculty is also called al-musawwira in other texts.
2
Understand its two operations: combining and separating
The text states 'tarkīb' (combination) and 'tafṣīl' (separation). Combining means putting images or meanings together; separating means breaking them apart. The example given is imagining a headless human – a clear case of separation.
The combination of images can produce many possibilities.
3
Recognize the two modes based on the controlling faculty
The passage says: when used by wahm (estimation), it is called mutakhayyila (imaginative); when used by aql (intellect), it is called mutafakkira (cogitative). This is the key distinction: the same faculty serves different roles depending on who employs it.
Mutakhayyila operates in dreams and instinctual imagery; mutafakkira aids rational thought.
4
Reflect on the significance of this distinction
Ask yourself: why does the author emphasize that the faculty is one but its names differ based on usage? This highlights that the internal senses are not fixed but can be harnessed by higher faculties for different purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتصرفة فهي قوة مرتبة في البطن الأوسط من الدماغ، من شأنها ترکیب بعض ما في الخيال أو الحافظة مع بعض، وتفصيله عنه. المتصرفة سميت بها؛ لكونها متصرفة في الصور الخيالية والمعاني الجزئية بالتركيب والتفصيل وتفصيله كأن يتصور إنسان بلا رأس وفي تركيب الصور والمعاني الجزئية، وتفصيلها احتمالات كثيرة، وهذه القوة المتصرفة إذا استعملها الوهم سمیت متخيلة، وإذا استعملها العقل سميت متفكرة.
As for the compositive faculty, it is a power located in the middle ventricle of the brain, its function being to combine some of what is in imagination or memory with others, and to separate it from it. The compositive faculty is so called because it disposes over imaginary forms and particular meanings by combination and separation. An example of separation is imagining a headless human, and in the combination of images and particular meanings there are many possibilities. This compositive faculty, when used by estimation, is called 'imaginative' (mutakhayyila), and when used by the intellect, it is called 'cogitative' (mutafakkira).
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the compositive faculty (al-mutasharrafa or al-musawwira) and explains its dual role. It is a power situated in the middle brain cavity. Its function is to combine (tarkib) or separate (tafsil) the contents of imagination (khayal) and memory (hafiza). The name 'mutasharrafa' indicates its ability to dispose over (tasrif) images and meanings. The author gives an example of separation: imagining a human without a head, which is not a real sensory image but a constructed one. This faculty has two modes depending on which higher faculty employs it. When used by the estimative faculty (wahm), it is called the imaginative faculty (mutakhayyila), responsible for forming images in dreams or instincts. When used by the intellect (aql), it is called the cogitative faculty (mutafakkira), which helps in intellectual reasoning by manipulating mental images. This distinction is important for understanding how internal senses serve both animal and human cognition.

Key Terms
المتصرفة (al-mutasharrafa): Faculty in the middle brain that combines and separates images and meanings; also called compositive imagination. [Note: In some texts, this faculty is called al-musawwira; here it is al-mutasharrafa, indicating its active manipulation.]
متخيلة (mutakhayyila): The compositive faculty when used by wahm; it produces images without rational control. [Note: In other works, al-mutakhayyila may be a distinct faculty; here it is a mode of al-mutasharrafa.]
متفكرة (mutafakkira): The compositive faculty when used by the intellect (aql); it aids in thinking by combining images meaningfully. [Note: This term is consistent in philosophical psychology.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Whether it combines or separates.
C) Whether it is used by wahm or aql.
D) The type of images it uses.

Key Takeaway
The compositive faculty is one, but its name and role change depending on whether it is commanded by estimation (yielding imagination) or intellect (yielding cogitation).

Motive Faculty: Impelling and Executive, Appetitive and Irascible
Defines motive faculty and its subdivisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two parts of the impelling motive faculty and distinguish between appetitive and irascible drives based on imagined benefit or harm.

Core Idea
Our actions are driven by seeking pleasure or repelling harm – the two branches of motive faculty.

The author explains how the motive faculty that moves the body is structured. It splits into an impelling part that decides to move and an executive part that carries out movement. The impelling part itself has two forms: appetitive (seeking benefit/pleasure) and irascible (repelling harm/seeking dominance). Understanding this helps us analyze the psychological roots of behaviour in Islamic psychology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main divisions
The motive faculty is first divided into impelling (ba‘itha) and executive (fa‘ila). Impelling initiates movement; executive executes it.
2
Understand the trigger of impelling
The impelling faculty is activated when an image of a desired or feared object appears in the imagination.
3
Differentiate appetitive vs irascible
If the movement seeks pleasure or benefit, it is appetitive (shahwaniyya). If it repels harm or seeks dominance, it is irascible (ghadabiyya).
4
Note the role of perception
The author emphasises that it is the soul's imagined perception of something as agreeable or harmful (not objective reality) that triggers the response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القوة المحركة فتنقسم إلى باعثة وفاعلة. أما الباعثة فهي القوة التي إذا ارتسمت في الخيال صورة مطلوبة أو مهروبة عنها حملت الفاعلة على التحريك وهي إن حملت الفاعلة على تحريك يطلب به الأشياء المتخيلة ضارة أو نافعة لحصول اللذة، تسمى قوة شهوانية، وإن حملت على تحريك يدفع به الشيء المتخيل ضارا أو مفيدا طالبا للغلبة، تسمى قوة غضبية. القوة المحركة وهي قوة مودعة في العضلات والأوتار. تحريك إلخ: أي إذا أذعنت النفس أن ذلك الشيء ملائم لها سواء كان في الواقع ملائما لها أو لا، تحرك الأعضاء بواسطة الباعثة الحاملة الفاعلة على تحريك يدفع به إلخ أي إذا تخيلت النفس أن ذلك الشيء ضارا لها، سواء كان في نفس الأمر ضارا لها أو لا، تحمل القوة الباعثة الفاعلة على تحريك الأعضاء؛ لتدفع به المؤذي وتتنقم منه.
As for the motive faculty, it is divided into an impelling (ba‘itha) and an executive (fa‘ila). As for the impelling, it is the faculty which, when a desired or fled-from image is impressed in the imagination, drives the executive to movement. And if it drives the executive to a movement by which it seeks the imagined things harmful or beneficial for attaining pleasure, it is called the appetitive (shahwaniyya) power; and if it drives to a movement by which it repels the imagined thing harmful or beneficial seeking dominance, it is called the irascible (ghadabiyya) power. The motive faculty: it is a power deposited in the muscles and tendons. 'Movement, etc.': that is, when the soul judges that something is agreeable to it, whether it is actually agreeable or not, it moves the limbs by means of the impelling which carries the executive to movement by which it repels, etc.; that is, when the soul imagines that something is harmful to it, whether in reality it is harmful or not, the impelling power carries the executive to move the limbs in order to repel the harmful thing and take vengeance upon it.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the motive faculty (al-quwwa al-muharrika) and analyzes its components. First, it is divided into two: the impelling (ba‘itha) and the executive (fa‘ila). The impelling is triggered when an image of a desired or feared object appears in the imagination; it then prompts the executive to move the body. Depending on the nature of the object, the impelling further subdivides into two: appetitive (shahwaniyya) when the movement seeks pleasure or benefit, and irascible (ghadabiyya) when the movement repels harm or seeks dominance. The author then adds a gloss explaining that the motive faculty resides in muscles and tendons, and that both appetitive and irascible responses are based on the soul's perception of something as agreeable or harmful, regardless of objective reality. This highlights the psychological basis of action. The classification is important for understanding how internal perceptions drive behaviour.

Key Terms

باعثة
فاعلة
شهوانية
غضبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The strength of the imagined image.
B) Whether the imagined object is beneficial or harmful.
C) Whether the movement is voluntary.
D) The part of the body moved.

Key Takeaway
The motive faculty divides into impelling and executive, and the impelling further divides into appetitive (seeking benefit) and irascible (repelling harm), based on imagined perceptions.

The Estimative Faculty: Apprehending Non-Sensible Meanings
Author defines the three internal faculties of animals: estimative, composing, and motor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the estimative faculty and identify its function and treasury from the passage.

Core Idea
The estimative faculty grasps non-sensible meanings within sensible objects.

The animal soul possesses internal faculties beyond the five senses. One is the estimative (wahm), which apprehends particular meanings like enmity in a wolf—meanings not directly sensed but attached to objects. These meanings are stored in a treasury, enabling later recognition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its definition
Find 'القوة الوهمية' in the Arabic and note its definition: it apprehends particular non-sensible meanings present in sensible objects.
2
Recognize the treasury (khazana)
The passage states that these meanings are stored in the treasury (خزانة) of the estimative faculty. Without this storage, we could not judge that a meaning perceived now is the same as one perceived before.
This shows the faculty is not just perceptive but also has a memory component.
3
Understand the epistemological implication
The author argues that if meanings were not preserved during heedlessness, judgment of identity would be impossible. This highlights the necessity of the treasury for coherent experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في العنصريات V. فصل في الحيوان ما تدركه القوة الوهمية من المعاني الجزئية الغير المحسوسة الموجودة في المحسوسات، وهي خزانة القوة الوهمية. وأما المتصرفة فهي قوة مرتبة في البطن الأوسط من الدماغ، من شأنها تركيب بعض ما في الخيال أو الحافظة مع بعض، وتفصيله عنه. وأما القوة المحركة فتنقسم إلى باعثة وفاعلة. أما الباعثة فهي القوة التي إذا ارتسمت في الخيال صورة مطلوبة أو مهروبة عنها حملت الفاعلة على التحريك وهي إن حملت الفاعلة على تحريك يطلب به الأشياء المتخيلة ضارة أو نافعة لحصول اللذة، تسمى قوة شهوانية، وإن حملت على تحريك يدفع به الشيء المتخيل ضارا أو مفيدا طالبا للغلبة، تسمى قوة غضبية. - نحكم أن تلك المعاني قد وجدناها فيها من قبل، فلو لم تكن تلك المعاني محفوظة فينا زمان الذهول لامتنع الحكم عليها أنها هي هي. المتصرفة سميت بها؛ لكونها متصرفة في الصور الخيالية والمعاني الجزئية بالتركيب والتفصيل وتفصيله كأن يتصور إنسان بلا رأس وفي تركيب الصور والمعاني الجزئية، وتفصيلها احتمالات كثيرة، وهذه القوة المتصرفة إذا استعملها الوهم سميت متخيلة، وإذا استعملها العقل سميت متفكرة. القوة المحركة وهي قوة مودعة في العضلات والأوتار. تحريك إلخ: أي إذا أذعنت النفس أن ذلك الشيء ملائم لها سواء كان في الواقع ملائما لها أو لا، تحرك الأعضاء بواسطة الباعثة الحاملة الفاعلة على تحريك يدفع به إلخ أي إذا تخيلت النفس أن ذلك الشيء ضارا لها، سواء كان في نفس الأمر ضارا لها أو لا، تحمل القوة الباعثة الفاعلة على تحريك الأعضاء؛ لتدفع به المؤذي وتتنقم منه.
The third art on elemental things. V. Chapter on the animal. What the estimative faculty apprehends of particular, non-sensible meanings present in sensible objects, and it is the treasury of the estimative faculty. As for the composing faculty, it is a faculty located in the middle ventricle of the brain, whose function is to combine some of what is in the imagination or memory with some, and to separate it from it. As for the motor faculty, it divides into inciting and executive. As for the inciting, it is the faculty that, when a desired or fled-from image is depicted in the imagination, impels the executive to movement. If it impels the executive to a movement by which it seeks imagined things harmful or beneficial for the attainment of pleasure, it is called the appetitive faculty; if it impels to a movement by which it repels the imagined thing harmful or beneficial, seeking domination, it is called the irascible faculty. - We judge that those meanings we have found in them before; for if those meanings were not preserved in us at the time of heedlessness, judgment that they are the same would be impossible. The composing faculty is named thus because it composes in imaginary forms and particular meanings by combination and separation, and its separation such as imagining a human without a head, and in the combination of forms and particular meanings and their separation there are many possibilities. This composing faculty, when used by the estimative, is called imaginative; when used by the intellect, it is called thinking. The motor faculty is a faculty deposited in the muscles and tendons. Movement etc.: i.e., when the self is convinced that a thing is agreeable to it, whether in reality it is agreeable or not, it moves the limbs by means of the inciting, which impels the executive to a movement by which it repels etc.: i.e., when the self imagines that a thing is harmful to it, whether in itself it is harmful or not, it impels the inciting faculty to move the limbs to repel the harm and take revenge.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines the three internal faculties of animals: the estimative (quwwa wahmiyya), the composing (quwwa mutasarrifa), and the motor (quwwa muharrika). The estimative faculty apprehends particular meanings not directly sensed, e.g., the meaning of enmity in a wolf. These meanings are stored in its treasury (khazana). The composing faculty, located in the middle ventricle of the brain, combines or separates images and meanings from the imagination (khayal) and memory (hafiza). When used by the estimative it is called imaginative (mutakhayyila); when used by the intellect it is called thinking (mutafakkira). An example given is imagining a headless human. The motor faculty is divided into inciting (ba'itha) and executive (fa'ila). The inciting faculty, upon perceiving a desired or feared image, drives the executive to move. If it drives toward perceived benefit for pleasure, it is appetitive (shahwaniyya); if it drives to repel harm or seek domination, it is irascible (ghadabiyya). The commentary explains that without a treasury storing meanings, we could not later judge that the same meanings were previously apprehended. This passage is definitional; it classifies the faculties and their functions within the animal soul.

خزانة

The Composing Faculty: Combining Images and Meanings
Author defines the three internal faculties of animals: estimative, composing, and motor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the composing faculty's location, function, and dual naming depending on its controller.

Core Idea
The composing faculty combines and separates images and meanings, called imaginative or thinking accordingly.

The animal soul has a faculty that manipulates contents of imagination and memory. The composing faculty (mutasarrifa) can create new images by combining or separating existing ones, e.g., a headless human. Its name changes based on whether the estimative (wahm) or intellect (aql) uses it: imaginative or thinking.

Guided Path
1
Locate the term and its definition
Find 'المتصرفة' in the Arabic and note its function: combining and separating what is in the imagination (خيال) and memory (حافظة).
The term is from ص-ر-ف, implying manipulation.
2
Identify its location
The passage places it in the middle ventricle of the brain: 'في البطن الأوسط من الدماغ'.
3
Examine the example
The example 'imagining a human without a head' shows how the faculty separates parts from wholes.
This illustrates its creative power.
4
Understand the dual naming
When used by the estimative (وهم), it is called imaginative (متخيلة); when used by the intellect (عقل), it is called thinking (متفكرة).
This links sensory-based cognition to rational thought.

Commentary
This passage defines the three internal faculties of animals: the estimative (quwwa wahmiyya), the composing (quwwa mutasarrifa), and the motor (quwwa muharrika). The estimative faculty apprehends particular meanings not directly sensed, e.g., the meaning of enmity in a wolf. These meanings are stored in its treasury (khazana). The composing faculty, located in the middle ventricle of the brain, combines or separates images and meanings from the imagination (khayal) and memory (hafiza). When used by the estimative it is called imaginative (mutakhayyila); when used by the intellect it is called thinking (mutafakkira). An example given is imagining a headless human. The motor faculty is divided into inciting (ba'itha) and executive (fa'ila). The inciting faculty, upon perceiving a desired or feared image, drives the executive to move. If it drives toward perceived benefit for pleasure, it is appetitive (shahwaniyya); if it drives to repel harm or seek domination, it is irascible (ghadabiyya). The commentary explains that without a treasury storing meanings, we could not later judge that the same meanings were previously apprehended. This passage is definitional; it classifies the faculties and their functions within the animal soul.

Key Terms
المتصرفة
متخيلة
متفكرة

Key Takeaway
The composing faculty's dual naming reflects the interface between sensory imagination and rational thought.

The Motor Faculty: Inciting and Executive Drives
Author defines the three internal faculties of animals: estimative, composing, and motor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the motor faculty's division into inciting and executive, and differentiate between appetitive and irascible drives.

Core Idea
The motor faculty's inciting part drives the executive to move toward pleasure or away from harm.

The motor faculty (muharrika), deposited in muscles and tendons, is divided into inciting (ba'itha) and executive (fa'ila). The inciting faculty, triggered by a desired or feared image, impels the executive to move. This can be appetitive (seeking pleasure) or irascible (seeking domination or repelling harm). These drives are based on the self's conviction of agreeableness or harm, regardless of reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the motor faculty and its division
Find 'القوة المحركة' and see it divides into باعثة (inciting) and فاعلة (executive).
2
Understand the inciting faculty's trigger
The inciting faculty is activated when a desired (مطلوبة) or fled-from (مهروبة) image is depicted in the imagination.
This shows it is responsive to both positive and negative stimuli.
3
Differentiate appetitive and irascible
If the movement seeks imagined benefit for pleasure, it is شهوانية (appetitive); if it seeks to repel harm or dominate, it is غضبية (irascible).
These correspond to desire and anger.
4
Note the self's conviction
The self's conviction (إذعان النفس) that something is agreeable or harmful drives the faculty, regardless of reality.
This explains subjective responses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث في العنصريات V. فصل في الحيوان ما تدركه القوة الوهمية من المعاني الجزئية الغير المحسوسة الموجودة في المحسوسات، وهي خزانة القوة الوهمية. وأما المتصرفة فهي قوة مرتبة في البطن الأوسط من الدماغ، من شأنها تركيب بعض ما في الخيال أو الحافظة مع بعض، وتفصيله عنه. وأما القوة المحركة فتنقسم إلى باعثة وفاعلة. أما الباعثة فهي القوة التي إذا ارتسمت في الخيال صورة مطلوبة أو مهروبة عنها حملت الفاعلة على التحريك وهي إن حملت الفاعلة على تحريك يطلب به الأشياء المتخيلة ضارة أو نافعة لحصول اللذة، تسمى قوة شهوانية، وإن حملت على تحريك يدفع به الشيء المتخيل ضارا أو مفيدا طالبا للغلبة، تسمى قوة غضبية. - نحكم أن تلك المعاني قد وجدناها فيها من قبل، فلو لم تكن تلك المعاني محفوظة فينا زمان الذهول لامتنع الحكم عليها أنها هي هي. المتصرفة سميت بها؛ لكونها متصرفة في الصور الخيالية والمعاني الجزئية بالتركيب والتفصيل وتفصيله كأن يتصور إنسان بلا رأس وفي تركيب الصور والمعاني الجزئية، وتفصيلها احتمالات كثيرة، وهذه القوة المتصرفة إذا استعملها الوهم سميت متخيلة، وإذا استعملها العقل سميت متفكرة. القوة المحركة وهي قوة مودعة في العضلات والأوتار. تحريك إلخ: أي إذا أذعنت النفس أن ذلك الشيء ملائم لها سواء كان في الواقع ملائما لها أو لا، تحرك الأعضاء بواسطة الباعثة الحاملة الفاعلة على تحريك يدفع به إلخ أي إذا تخيلت النفس أن ذلك الشيء ضارا لها، سواء كان في نفس الأمر ضارا لها أو لا، تحمل القوة الباعثة الفاعلة على تحريك الأعضاء؛ لتدفع به المؤذي وتتنقم منه.
The third art on elemental things. V. Chapter on the animal. What the estimative faculty apprehends of particular, non-sensible meanings present in sensible objects, and it is the treasury of the estimative faculty. As for the composing faculty, it is a faculty located in the middle ventricle of the brain, whose function is to combine some of what is in the imagination or memory with some, and to separate it from it. As for the motor faculty, it divides into inciting and executive. As for the inciting, it is the faculty that, when a desired or fled-from image is depicted in the imagination, impels the executive to movement. If it impels the executive to a movement by which it seeks imagined things harmful or beneficial for the attainment of pleasure, it is called the appetitive faculty; if it impels to a movement by which it repels the imagined thing harmful or beneficial, seeking domination, it is called the irascible faculty. - We judge that those meanings we have found in them before; for if those meanings were not preserved in us at the time of heedlessness, judgment that they are the same would be impossible. The composing faculty is named thus because it composes in imaginary forms and particular meanings by combination and separation, and its separation such as imagining a human without a head, and in the combination of forms and particular meanings and their separation there are many possibilities. This composing faculty, when used by the estimative, is called imaginative; when used by the intellect, it is called thinking. The motor faculty is a faculty deposited in the muscles and tendons. Movement etc.: i.e., when the self is convinced that a thing is agreeable to it, whether in reality it is agreeable or not, it moves the limbs by means of the inciting, which impels the executive to a movement by which it repels etc.: i.e., when the self imagines that a thing is harmful to it, whether in itself it is harmful or not, it impels the inciting faculty to move the limbs to repel the harm and take revenge.

Defining the Rational Soul and Its Powers
Author defines the rational soul, its two powers, and the first of its four stages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of the rational soul and distinguish its two powers.

Core Idea
The rational soul is defined by its genus (first perfection) and differentia (apprehending universals).

This passage provides a foundational definition of the human rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) in Islamic philosophy. Understanding this definition is crucial because it establishes the soul's nature as a substance that is both cognitive and active. We will walk through the key components: the definition itself, the two powers (rational and practical), and the first stage of the rational power. A commentary also clarifies an important epistemological distinction between informational knowledge and scientific knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the definition framework
The passage begins by stating that the human is specifically characterized by the rational soul. Note that the definition uses the form 'first perfection of a natural organic body', standard for defining the soul in the Peripatetic tradition.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'first perfection of a natural organic body' (what category does the soul belong to?). The differentia is 'insofar as it apprehends universal matters and performs intellectual actions' (what distinguishes it from other perfections?). Write them down.
3
Distinguish the two powers
The author divides the soul's faculties into a rational power (for apprehending conceptualizations and assents) and a practical power (for moving the body to particular actions). Note the functions of each.
4
Locate the first stage of the rational power
The first of four stages is described: the soul is empty of all intelligibles but has a preparation for them. This is the material intellect (al-ʿaql al-hayūlānī).
5
Understand the commentary's clarification on conceptualizations and assents
The commentary stresses that the terms 'conceptualizations and assents' refer to informational content (maʿlūmāt), not the sciences (ʿulūm) themselves. This distinction is important because the soul's self-knowledge is presentational, not acquisitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الإنسان هو مختص بالنفس الناطقة، وهي كمال أول لجسم طبعي آلي من جهة ما تدرك الأمور الكلية وتفعل الأفعال الفكرية، فلها قوة عاقلة تدرك بها التصورات والتصديقات، وقوة عاملة تحرك بها بدن الإنسان إلى الأفعال الجزئية بالفكر والروية على مقتضى آراء تخصها، والنفس باعتبار القوة العاقلة لها مراتب أربع المرتبة الأولى: أن تكون خالية عن جميع المعقولات، بل هي مستعدة لها. العضلات وهي جمع عضلة، وهي جسم مركب من العصب والرباط واللحم، والعصب: جسم أبيض ينبت من الدماغ والنخاع والرباط: جسم أبيض ينبت من العظم، فذانك الجسمان يختلفان ويبقى بينهما فرج، ويملأها لحم فتحصل منها العضلة، وفيها قوة محركة للأعضاء قبضا وبسطا، وفي الأعضاء التي ليست فيها العضلات لا يقدر الإنسان على تحريكها كأذني الإنسان بخلاف الحيوان. بالنفس الناطقة: وهي جوهر مجرد عن المادة في ذاته متعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف تسمى بالنفس؛ لتعلقها بالبدن وناطقة لكونها مدركة الكليات. التصورات إلخ المراد بالتصورات والتصديقات المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لا العلوم التصورية والتصديقية، وإلا لزم أن تكون النفس تدرك بتلك القوة العلوم، وقد تقرر في مقامه أن علم العلم حضوري لا حصولي، وتلك القوة إنما هي للحصولي لا للحضوري؛ لأنه حاضر بنفسه عند العالم فكيف يحتاج إلى القوة العاقلة؟ المعقولات إنما قال المعقولات وهي صور الأشياء؛ لأن النفس لا تخلو عن علمها وصفاتها، وعلم النفس بصفاتها حضوري لا حصولي.
Chapter on the human. He is specifically characterized by the rational soul, which is the first perfection of a natural organic body, insofar as it apprehends universal matters and performs intellectual actions. For it has a rational power by which it apprehends conceptualizations and assents, and a practical power by which it moves the human body to particular actions through thought and deliberation according to opinions specific to it. The soul, in respect of the rational power, has four stages. The first stage: that it be empty of all intelligibles, rather it is prepared for them. The muscles: plural of muscle, which is a body composed of nerve, ligament, and flesh. The nerve: a white body that grows from the brain and spinal cord. The ligament: a white body that grows from bone. Those two bodies differ and between them remains a gap, and flesh fills it, so the muscle results. In it is a motor power for limbs by contraction and expansion, and in limbs that have no muscles, the human cannot move them, like the ears of a human, unlike animals. By the rational soul: it is a substance abstracted from matter in its essence, related to the body by the relation of management and disposal; it is called 'soul' because of its relation to the body, and 'rational' because it apprehends universals. 'Conceptualizations' etc.: what is meant by conceptualizations and assents is informational conceptualizations and assents, not the sciences of conceptualizations and assents; otherwise it would follow that the soul apprehends sciences by that power, and it has been established in its place that knowledge of knowledge is presentational, not acquisitive, and that power is only for acquisitive, not presentational, because it is present by itself to the knower, so how could it need the rational power? 'Intelligibles': he only said 'intelligibles' and they are the forms of things, because the soul is never devoid of knowledge of itself and its attributes, and the soul's knowledge of its attributes is presentational, not acquisitive.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the rational soul (nafs natiqa) as the first perfection of a natural organic body, with the capacity to apprehend universals and perform intellectual actions. The soul has two powers: a rational power (quwwa aqila) for conceptualizations (tasawwurat) and assents (tasdiqat), and a practical power (quwwa amila) to move the body toward particular actions through thought and deliberation. The rational power has four stages; only the first stage is mentioned: complete potentiality without any actual intelligibles. This first stage is the 'material intellect' (al-'aql al-hayulani), a pure preparation. The accompanying commentary explains the composition of muscles and the nature of the rational soul as a substance abstract from matter but related to the body in governance. It distinguishes between informational knowledge (ma'lumat) and sciences ('ulum), stressing that the soul's knowledge of its own attributes is presentational (huduri) not acquisitive (husuli), so the rational power is needed only for acquisitive knowledge. The key point is that the intelligibles (ma'qulat) are forms of things, and the soul's self-knowledge is immediate.

The Rational Soul as the First Perfection of the Body
Definition of the rational soul as first perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the genus and differentia in the definition of the rational soul and explain why it is called 'first perfection'.

Core Idea
The rational soul is not a part of the body but its form – the first perfection that makes it human.

In this passage, the author gives a precise definition of the rational soul using the Aristotelian concept of 'first perfection' (kamāl awwal). Understanding this definition requires seeing the soul as the actualizing principle of a natural organic body, distinct from the body itself and from any accidental perfections.

Guided Path
1
Find the definiendum
Identify the term being defined – 'al-nafs al-nāṭiqa' (the rational soul). The passage begins by stating that man is distinguished by it.
2
Extract the genus
The genus is 'first perfection' (kamāl awwal). Ask: what does 'first perfection' mean? It is the form that makes a body belong to its species.
3
Identify the differentia
The differentia is 'in that it apprehends universal matters and performs intellectual acts'. This distinguishes the rational soul from other perfections like the animal soul.
4
Check the definition's completeness
Does it cover all humans and only humans? Yes, because rationality is the defining characteristic of humanity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الإنسان هو مختص بالنفس الناطقة، وهي كمال أول لجسم طبعي آلي من جهة ما تدرك الأمور الكلية وتفعل الأفعال الفكرية، فلها قوة عاقلة تدرك بها التصورات والتصديقات، وقوة عاملة تحرك بها بدن الإنسان إلى الأفعال الجزئية بالفكر والروية على مقتضى آراء تخصها. النفس الناطقة: وهي جوهر مجرد عن المادة في ذاته متعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف تسمى بالنفس؛ لتعلقها بالبدن وناطقة لكونها مدركة الكليات. التصورات إلخ المراد بالتصورات والتصديقات المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لا العلوم التصورية والتصديقية، وإلا لزم أن تكون النفس تدرك بتلك القوة العلوم، وقد تقرر في مقامه أن علم العلم حضوري لا حصولي، وتلك القوة إنما هي للحصولي لا للحضوري؛ لأنه حاضر بنفسه عند العالم فكيف يحتاج إلى القوة العاقلة؟ المعقولات إنما قال المعقولات وهي صور الأشياء؛ لأن النفس لا تخلو عن علمها وصفاتها، وعلم النفس بصفاتها حضوري لا حصولي.
Chapter on Man. He is distinguished by the rational soul, which is the first perfection of a natural organic body in that it apprehends universal matters and performs intellectual acts. It has a cognitive power by which it apprehends conceptualizations and assents, and a practical power by which it moves the human body to particular actions through thought and deliberation in accordance with its own opinions. 'Rational soul': it is a substance abstract from matter in itself, attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition, called 'soul' due to its attachment to the body and 'rational' for being the apprehender of universals. 'Conceptualizations etc.': what is meant by conceptualizations and assents are conceptual and assertive informations, not conceptual and assertive sciences; otherwise the soul would apprehend sciences by that power, and it has been established in its place that knowledge of science is presential, not acquired, and that power is only for the acquired, not for the presential, because it is present itself to the knower, so how does it need the cognitive power? 'Intelligibles': he only said 'intelligibles' which are the forms of things, because the soul is not devoid of knowledge of itself and its attributes, and the soul's knowledge of its attributes is presential, not acquired.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) and its two main faculties. The author begins by stating that man is distinguished by the rational soul, which is described as the first perfection (kamāl awwal) of a natural organic body. 'First perfection' means the form that makes a body actually human, as opposed to a second perfection like the ability to walk or see. The soul acts through two aspects: it apprehends universals (al-umūr al-kulliyya) and performs intellectual acts (al-afʿāl al-fikriyya). To this end, it has two powers: the cognitive power (al-quwwa al-ʿāqila) which apprehends conceptualizations (taṣawwurāt) and assents (taṣdīqāt), and the practical power (al-quwwa al-ʿāmila) which moves the body to particular actions through thought and deliberation (al-fikr wa al-rawwiyya) in accordance with its own opinions. Then the author provides a detailed gloss on 'rational soul': it is a substance abstract from matter in itself, attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition (tadabbur and taṣarruf). It is called 'soul' because of its attachment to the body, and 'rational' because it apprehends universals. He also clarifies that the conceptualizations and assents referred to are informational (maʿlūmāt), not sciences (ʿulūm), because the soul's knowledge of its own science is presential (ḥuḍūrī), not acquired (ḥuṣūlī), so it cannot be the object of the cognitive power which is for acquired rather than presential knowledge. Finally, he notes that 'intelligibles' (al-maʿqūlāt) are the forms of things; the soul always has knowledge of itself and its attributes, which is presential, so that knowledge is not subject to the cognitive power.

The Two Powers of the Rational Soul
Division of the rational soul into cognitive and practical powers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the cognitive and practical powers of the rational soul based on their functions.

Core Idea
The rational soul acts through two powers: one to know universals, another to move the body in particular actions.

Having defined the rational soul, the author now divides it into two faculties: the cognitive power (quwwa ʿāqila) and the practical power (quwwa ʿāmila). The first deals with abstract knowledge, the second with bodily actions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two powers
Look for 'قوة عاقلة' and 'قوة عاملة' in the text.
2
Describe the cognitive power
It apprehends conceptualizations (tasawwurāt) and assents (tasdiqāt). This is the theoretical intellect.
3
Describe the practical power
It moves the body to particular actions through thought and deliberation (al-fikr wa al-rawwiyya). It follows its own opinions (ārā').
4
Compare the objects
Cognitive power deals with universals; practical power deals with particular actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الإنسان هو مختص بالنفس الناطقة، وهي كمال أول لجسم طبعي آلي من جهة ما تدرك الأمور الكلية وتفعل الأفعال الفكرية، فلها قوة عاقلة تدرك بها التصورات والتصديقات، وقوة عاملة تحرك بها بدن الإنسان إلى الأفعال الجزئية بالفكر والروية على مقتضى آراء تخصها. النفس الناطقة: وهي جوهر مجرد عن المادة في ذاته متعلق بالبدن تعلق التدبير والتصرف تسمى بالنفس؛ لتعلقها بالبدن وناطقة لكونها مدركة الكليات. التصورات إلخ المراد بالتصورات والتصديقات المعلومات التصورية والتصديقية، لا العلوم التصورية والتصديقية، وإلا لزم أن تكون النفس تدرك بتلك القوة العلوم، وقد تقرر في مقامه أن علم العلم حضوري لا حصولي، وتلك القوة إنما هي للحصولي لا للحضوري؛ لأنه حاضر بنفسه عند العالم فكيف يحتاج إلى القوة العاقلة؟ المعقولات إنما قال المعقولات وهي صور الأشياء؛ لأن النفس لا تخلو عن علمها وصفاتها، وعلم النفس بصفاتها حضوري لا حصولي.
Chapter on Man. He is distinguished by the rational soul, which is the first perfection of a natural organic body in that it apprehends universal matters and performs intellectual acts. It has a cognitive power by which it apprehends conceptualizations and assents, and a practical power by which it moves the human body to particular actions through thought and deliberation in accordance with its own opinions. 'Rational soul': it is a substance abstract from matter in itself, attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition, called 'soul' due to its attachment to the body and 'rational' for being the apprehender of universals. 'Conceptualizations etc.': what is meant by conceptualizations and assents are conceptual and assertive informations, not conceptual and assertive sciences; otherwise the soul would apprehend sciences by that power, and it has been established in its place that knowledge of science is presential, not acquired, and that power is only for the acquired, not for the presential, because it is present itself to the knower, so how does it need the cognitive power? 'Intelligibles': he only said 'intelligibles' which are the forms of things, because the soul is not devoid of knowledge of itself and its attributes, and the soul's knowledge of its attributes is presential, not acquired.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines the rational soul (al-nafs al-nāṭiqa) and its two main faculties. The author begins by stating that man is distinguished by the rational soul, which is described as the first perfection (kamāl awwal) of a natural organic body. 'First perfection' means the form that makes a body actually human, as opposed to a second perfection like the ability to walk or see. The soul acts through two aspects: it apprehends universals (al-umūr al-kulliyya) and performs intellectual acts (al-afʿāl al-fikriyya). To this end, it has two powers: the cognitive power (al-quwwa al-ʿāqila) which apprehends conceptualizations (taṣawwurāt) and assents (taṣdīqāt), and the practical power (al-quwwa al-ʿāmila) which moves the body to particular actions through thought and deliberation (al-fikr wa al-rawwiyya) in accordance with its own opinions. Then the author provides a detailed gloss on 'rational soul': it is a substance abstract from matter in itself, attached to the body with the attachment of management and disposition (tadabbur and taṣarruf). It is called 'soul' because of its attachment to the body, and 'rational' because it apprehends universals. He also clarifies that the conceptualizations and assents referred to are informational (maʿlūmāt), not sciences (ʿulūm), because the soul's knowledge of its own science is presential (ḥuḍūrī), not acquired (ḥuṣūlī), so it cannot be the object of the cognitive power which is for acquired rather than presential knowledge. Finally, he notes that 'intelligibles' (al-maʿqūlāt) are the forms of things; the soul always has knowledge of itself and its attributes, which is presential, so that knowledge is not subject to the cognitive power.

قوة عاملة

Conceptualizations as Informations, Not Sciences
Clarification that conceptualizations are informations, not sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the author distinguishes 'informations' (maʿlūmāt) from 'sciences' (ʿulūm) in the context of the cognitive power.

Core Idea
The soul's knowledge of its own knowledge is presential, not acquired; thus the cognitive power deals only with acquired concepts and judgments.

After defining the two powers, the author provides a crucial clarification: when he says the cognitive power apprehends conceptualizations and assents, he means the informations (maʿlūmāt) that are acquired, not the sciences themselves (ʿulūm). This is because the soul's knowledge of its own science is presential (ḥuḍūrī), not representationally acquired.

Guided Path
1
Identify the apparent problem
If the cognitive power apprehends sciences, then the soul would have acquired knowledge of its own science, but that is impossible because science is present to the soul.
2
Understand the distinction
The text distinguishes between 'conceptual and assertive informations' (maʿlūmāt taṣawwuriyya wa taṣdīqiyya) and 'conceptual and assertive sciences' (ʿulūm).
3
Apply the presential/acquired distinction
Knowledge of science is presential (ḥuḍūrī), not acquired (ḥuṣūlī). The cognitive power is only for acquired knowledge.
4
See the consequence
Therefore, what the cognitive power apprehends are the informational contents (maʿlūmāt), not the sciences themselves.

The Four Stages of the Theoretical Intellect
Author defines the four ascending stages of the theoretical intellect with explanations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and describe the four ascending stages of the theoretical intellect as defined in Islamic philosophy.

Core Idea
The intellect's four stages map the soul's journey from pure potentiality to contemplative perfection.

In Islamic philosophy, the theoretical intellect (العقل النظري) is the faculty by which the soul grasps universal truths. The passage before us outlines its four progressive stages, from raw receptivity to complete actualization. Understanding these stages is essential for grasping how knowledge is acquired in the Aristotelian-Farabian tradition adopted by Muslim philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the First Stage: Hylic Intellect
Find the term 'العقل الهيولاني' in the Arabic and note its definition: pure potentiality for intelligible forms, analogous to prime matter (hyle).
The name comes from 'هيولى' (hyle), emphasizing formless receptivity.
2
Understand the Second Stage: Intellect in Habitus
Locate 'العقل بالملكة' and see that it possesses axiomatic intelligibles (بديهيات) and a firm disposition (ملكة) to infer theoretical knowledge.
This stage is a stable readiness, not yet active contemplation.
3
Distinguish the Third Stage: Actual Intellect
Find 'العقل بالفعل'—here the intelligibles are stored but not actively contemplated; they are present but not in use.
Contrast: stored vs. actively contemplated.
4
Analyze the Fourth Stage: Absolute Intellect
Examine 'العقل المطلق' (also called 'العقل المستفاد'): the intellect actively contemplates all its acquired intelligibles, a state typically achieved after death.
The passage notes this perfect state is attained in the afterlife, except for rare pure souls.
5
Recognize the Sacred Power
Note the special term 'القوة القدسية' for the intellect in habitus at its utmost perfection—a sacred or holy power.
This term links to prophetic or saintly intellects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الإنسان وهي العقل الهيولاني، والمرتبة الثانية أن تحصل لها المعقولات البديهية وتستعد لأن تنتقل من البديهيات إلى النظريات، وهي العقل بالملكة والمرتبة الثالثة أن تحصل لها المعقولات لكن لا تطالعها بالفعل بل صارت مخزونة عندها وهي العقل بالفعل، والمرتبة الرابعة: أن تطالع معقولاتها المكتسبة وهي العقل المطلق، وتسمى معقولاتها عقلا مستفادا، ثم العقل بالملكة إن كان في الغاية تسمى قوة قدسية. العقل الهيولاني: سمي به تشبيها بالهيولى، كما أن الهيولى في حد ذاتها خالية عن جميع الصور ومستعد لها كذلك العقل في هذه المرتبة. المعقولات البديهية: السرفيه أن النفس إذا بلغت سن التمييز وأحست جزئيات كثيرة من أنواع مختلفة، تنبهت بملاحظة نسبة بعضها إلى بعض لجهة الاتحاد والاختلاف فاض إليها من المبدأ الفياض صورها الكلية حتى امتاز عندها نوع من نوع آخر، ثم تترقى حتى تستعد لأن تنتقل من الصور البديهية إلى النظريات. وهي العقل بالملكة: أي المرتبة الثانية من العقل تسمى العقل بالملكة؛ لما أن الكيفية الراسخة للانتقال إلى النظريات حصلت للنفس، والملكة هي الكيفية الراسخة. معقولاتها المكتسبة : المعقولات المكتسبة سواء كانت بديهيات أو نظريات، ومطالعة النفس بجميع معقولاتها إنما تكون في الدار الآخرة بعد المفارقة عن البدن، لا في الدنيا؛ لأن هناك موانع وصوارف من الأشغال الجسمانية، اللهم إلا في بعض النفوس الصافية. العقل المطلق لما كانت المعقولات تطالعها النفس صار العقل في هذه المرتبة کاملا، فإذا أطلق العقل يتبادر منه هذا العقل، فلذا سمي به. القوة العاقلة : المراد بها النفس الناطقة، وهي جوهر مجرد في ذاته متعلق بالبدن تعلق التصرف والتدبير.
Chapter on the Human Being. And it is the hylic intellect. The second rank: that it obtains the axiomatic intelligibles and is prepared to transition from axiomatics to theoreticals, and it is the intellect in habitus. The third rank: that it obtains the intelligibles but does not contemplate them actually, rather they become stored with it, and it is the actual intellect. The fourth rank: that it contemplates its acquired intelligibles, and it is the absolute intellect; its intelligibles are called derived intellect. Then the intellect in habitus, if it is at the utmost, is called sacred power. The hylic intellect: it is named by analogy to hyle, just as hyle in itself is devoid of all forms and prepared for them, so too the intellect in this rank. The axiomatic intelligibles: [Explanation] that when the soul reaches the age of discrimination and perceives many particulars of different kinds, it awakens by observing the relation of some to others in terms of unity and difference, then there flow to it from the effusive principle their universal forms until a species is distinguished from another species, then it progresses until it is prepared to transition from axiomatic forms to theoreticals. 'And it is the intellect in habitus': i.e., the second rank of intellect is called the intellect in habitus because the firm quality for transition to theoreticals has occurred to the soul, and habitus is the firm quality. Its acquired intelligibles: the acquired intelligibles, whether axiomatic or theoretical, and the soul's contemplation of all its intelligibles will only be in the afterlife after separation from the body, not in this world, because there are obstacles and diversions from bodily preoccupations, except in some pure souls. The absolute intellect: when the soul contemplates the intelligibles, the intellect in this rank becomes perfect; thus if the intellect is mentioned absolutely, this intellect is understood, so it is named by it. The rational faculty: what is intended by it is the rational soul, and it is an immaterial substance in itself, attached to the body in terms of managing and directing.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the four stages of the theoretical intellect (العقل النظري) as understood in the Islamic philosophical tradition, particularly following the Peripatetic framework. The text begins by listing the ranks: first the hylic intellect (العقل الهيولاني), which is pure potentiality for intelligible forms, analogous to prime matter (hyle). Second, the intellect in habitus (العقل بالملكة), which has acquired the first principles (بديهيات) and is ready to infer theoretical knowledge from them. Third, the actual intellect (العقل بالفعل), when the intelligibles are possessed but not actively contemplated — they are stored. Fourth, the absolute intellect (العقل المطلق), which actively contemplates its acquired intelligibles. The commentary then explains each term. It notes that the hylic intellect is called so by analogy with hyle, which is formless but receptive. The axiomatic intelligibles are innate principles that arise in the discerning soul through experience with particulars, and from these the soul progresses to theoreticals. The intellect in habitus is so named because it has a firm disposition (ملكة) for transition to theoreticals. Acquired intelligibles can be axiomatic or theoretical, and full contemplation occurs after death, except for rare pure souls. The absolute intellect is the perfect stage where the intellect attains completeness. Finally, the rational faculty (القوة العاقلة) is identified with the rational soul (النفس الناطقة), an immaterial substance that manages the body. This classification is foundational for understanding epistemology and psychology in Islamic philosophy.

Key Terms
العقل الهيولاني
العقل بالملكة
العقل بالفعل
العقل المطلق
القوة القدسية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) العقل الهيولاني
B) العقل بالملكة
C) العقل بالفعل
D) العقل المطلق

Key Takeaway
The theoretical intellect ascends from pure potentiality to active contemplation, each stage representing a greater actualization of knowledge.
