Definition and Disjunctive Proof of Power (Quwwa)
Defines power and proves its existence via disjunctive syllogism with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to (1) identify the definition of quwwa as the principle of change in another, and (2) reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism proving that regular bodily effects require an inherent power.

Core Idea
Power is the principle of change in another; its existence is proven by eliminating bodyhood and accidents as causes of regular effects.

This lesson examines the definition of power (quwwa) and the proof of its existence in bodies. The author defines quwwa as the principle of change in another, with a crucial qualification to account for cases like the soul acting on itself. Then he presents a disjunctive syllogism to show that the regular effects we observe (place, quality, motion, rest) must come from an inherent power, not from mere corporeality or accidental matters. This proof is a classic example of reasoning by elimination (sabr wa taqsim). The commentary also clarifies the distinction between quwwa as principle of change and quwwa as potentiality (isti'dad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of quwwa
Find the sentence defining quwwa and note the qualification 'insofar as it is another'.
The qualification distinguishes cases where agent and patient are identical in essence but different in consideration.
2
List the three possibilities for the cause of bodily effects
The author gives three possibilities: bodyhood, accidental matters, or an inherent power. Write them down.
3
Assess the elimination of the first two possibilities
Why is bodyhood rejected? Because all bodies would share the same effects. Why are accidental matters rejected? Because they are neither permanent nor prevalent.
This elimination uses empirical observation and the principle of regularity.
4
Draw the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is a power existing in the body. This concludes the proof.
This is a valid disjunctive syllogism (modus tollendo ponens).
5
Distinguish between the two senses of quwwa
The commentary explains that the quwwa discussed here (principle of change) is not the same as quwwa as potentiality (isti'dad). Understand the difference.
The second sense is the disposition of matter to receive forms, often contrasted with actuality (fi'l).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في القوة والفعل
القوة هي الشيء الذي هو مبدأ التغير في آخر من حيث هو آخر، وكل ما يصدر عن الأجسام في العادة المستمرة المحسوسة من الآثار والأفعال كالاختصاص بـ "أين" و"كيف" و "حركة" و "سكون" فهي صادرة عن قوة موجودة فيه؛ لأن ذلك إما أن يكون لكونه جسما أو لأمور اتفاقية أو لقوة موجودة فيه، والأول باطل وإلا لاشتركت الأجسام فيه، والثاني أيضا باطل وإلا لما كان ذلك مستمرا ولا أكثريا؛ لأن الأمور الاتفاقية لا تكون دائمة ولا أكثرية، فإذن هو عن قوة موجودة فيه، وهو المطلوب.
وهو المادة: اعلم أن الحادث قد يكون جوهرا متعلقا بالمادة كالنفس الناطقة، وقد يكون جوهرا حالا فيها كالصورة النوعية، وقد يكون عرضا كالسواد وغيره، وإمكان الحدوث للكل عارض للمادة، وعند الفلاسفة مناط الحدوث استعداد المادة المتعاقب عليها؛ لتجدد الأوضاع الفلكية في القوة كان على المصنف محليه الاقتصار على القوة؛ لأن القوة التي ذكرها هي بمعنى مبدأ التغير وليست مقابلة للفعل، والقوة التي تقال بمقابلة الفعل معناها استعداد المادة، ولم يذكرها.
•
من حيث إنما قيده بالحيثية؛ لأن الفاعل والمفعول قد يكونان متحدين بالذات ومتغايرين بالاعتبار، كما في معالجة النفس لنفسها في الأمراض النفسانية.
لأن الأمور إلخ قيل عليه إن أراد بها مطلق الأمور الخارجة فلا نسلم أن الأمور الخارجة لا تكون دائمة ولا أكثرية؛ الجواز أن يكون بعض الأمور الخارجة دائمة وأكثرية، وإن أراد بها أن الأمور الاتفاقية تطلق على الأسباب التي لا تكون دائمة ولا أكثرية، فالحصر ممنوع؛ لجواز أن تكون تلك الآثار من أمور خارجة تكون -
Section on Power and Act. Power is the thing that is the principle of change in another insofar as it is another. And every effect and action that issues from bodies in the continuous customary sensible way, such as being in a place, quality, motion, and rest, these issue from a power existing in it. For that is either due to its being a body, or due to accidental matters, or due to a power existing in it. The first is false, otherwise all bodies would share it. The second is also false, otherwise it would not be continuous nor prevalent, because accidental matters are neither permanent nor prevalent. Therefore, it is due to a power existing in it, and that is what is sought. And that is the matter: Know that the originated may be a substance connected to matter, like the rational soul; or a substance inhering in it, like the specific form; or an accident, like blackness and others. And the possibility of origination for all of them is accidental to matter. According to the philosophers, the criterion of origination is the disposition of matter that successively [receives forms] due to the renewal of celestial positions. In potential (fi al-quwwa), it was upon the author to confine [the discussion] to power, because the power he mentioned is in the sense of the principle of change, not contrasted with act; and the power that is said in contrast to act means the disposition of matter, and he did not mention it. • The restriction 'insofar as it is another' is because the agent and the patient may be identical in essence but different in consideration, as in the soul treating itself in psychological illnesses. 'Because the matters' etc.: An objection was raised against it: If he means absolute external matters, we do not concede that external matters are not permanent or prevalent; it is possible that some external matters are permanent and prevalent. If he means that accidental matters refer to causes that are not permanent or prevalent, then the [exhaustive] division is rejected because it is possible that those effects come from external matters that are —
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage defines power (quwwa) and provides a proof for its existence in bodies. The definition states that power is the principle of change in another, with the qualification 'insofar as it is another' to distinguish cases where agent and patient are identical in essence (like the soul treating itself). The proof then argues that regular, observable effects (such as location, quality, motion, rest) must come from an inherent power, not from mere corporeality (which would make all bodies have the same effects) nor from accidental causes (which are not continuous or prevalent). This is a disjunctive syllogism: effects are either from bodyhood, accidents, or a power; the first two are false, so the third is true. The commentary then explains 'that is the matter', linking the concept to the earlier discussion of possibility and matter. It clarifies that the originated (hadith) can be a substance tied to matter (like the rational soul), a substance inhering in it (like substantial form), or an accident (like color). The possibility of origination for all is accidental to matter, and according to philosophers, the criterion of origination is the disposition of matter under celestial influences. The author is then criticized for only discussing power as the principle of change (not as opposed to act) while the disposition (isti'dad) is the power in the sense opposite to act. A further objection is raised against the proof: if 'accidental matters' means external factors, some may be permanent; if it means non-permanent causes, the division is not exhaustive because effects could come from permanent external factors. This objection is left incomplete in the text.

مبدأ التغير

Key Takeaway

?

The Four Causes in Islamic Philosophy
Definition of cause and its four types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four causes in any given effect and distinguish material from formal cause based on necessity of existence.

Core Idea
Material cause is the substrate; formal cause is the essence that makes it actually exist.

This passage introduces the concept of cause (علة) and its four types in the Aristotelian tradition as adopted by Islamic philosophers. Understanding these four causes is essential for analyzing arguments about creation, change, and purpose.

Guided Path
1
1. Understand the general definition of cause
Read the opening definition: cause is something that itself exists and from its existence the existence of another follows. This is a broad definition covering all four causes.
2
2. Identify the four types enumerated
The passage states there are four divisions: material, formal, efficient, and final. Memorize these in Arabic: المادية, الصورية, الفاعلية, الغائية.
3
3. Distinguish material from formal cause
Notice the key difference: material cause is a part but does not necessitate actual existence (e.g., clay alone does not make a pot), while formal cause does necessitate actual existence (the shape makes it a pot).
4
4. Recognize efficient and final causes
Efficient cause is the agent that brings about the effect (potter), final cause is the purpose or goal for which the effect exists (storage). Both are external to the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العلة والمعلول العلة تقال لكل ما له وجود في نفسه، ثم يحصل من وجوده وجود غيره، وهي أربعة أقسام مادية وصورية وفاعلية وغائية. أما المادية فهي التي تكون جزءا من المعلول، لكن لا يجب بها أن يكون موجودا بالفعل كالطين للكوز، وأما العلة الصورية فهي التي تكون جزءا من المعلول، لكن يجب بها أن يكون المعلول موجودا بالفعل كالصورة للكوز، وأما الفاعلية فهي التي يكون منها وجود المعلول كالفاعل للكوز، وأما الغائية فهي التي لأجلها وجود المعلول كالغرض المطلوب من الكوز. ثم العلة الفاعلية متى كانت بسيطة استحال دائمة وأكثرية. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه أن الكلام في الآثار التي تصدر عن الأجسام حين تجردها عن الأمور الخارجة، فلا يمكن حينئذ استنادها إلى أسباب خارجة، وهذا الجواب لا يلائم سوق كلام المصنف ؛ لذكره في الدليل أمورا اتفاقية. تقال إلخ : قيل عليه إن هذا التعريف لا يصدق إلا على العلة الفاعلية، وأجيب عنه: أن المراد أن يكون لوجود غيره حاجة إلى وجوده في الجملة والأولى أن تفسر العلة بما يحتاج إليه شيء في وجوده حتى يتناول العلة العدمية أيضا، ولا يحتاج في الجواب إلى التكلف بإرجاع العلة العدمية إلى العلة الوجودية وهي أربعة إلخ: قيل: الحصر منقوض بالشرط والمعد وعدم المانع، وأجيب عنه: أن المقسم هو علة الشيء بلا واسطة، وأقسامه أربعة والشرط والمعد وعدم المانع ليست علة بالذات.
Chapter on cause and effect. 'Cause' is said of everything that has existence in itself, then from its existence the existence of another arises. It has four divisions: material, formal, efficient, and final. As for the material cause, it is that which is a part of the effect, but it is not necessary that by it the effect exists in act, like clay for a pot. As for the formal cause, it is that which is a part of the effect, but it is necessary that by it the effect exists in act, like the shape for a pot. As for the efficient cause, it is that from which the existence of the effect comes, like the potter for a pot. As for the final cause, it is that for the sake of which the existence of the effect is, like the purpose sought from the pot. Then the efficient cause, when it is simple, cannot be perpetual or multiple. It can be answered that the discussion is about the effects that proceed from bodies when they are stripped of external matters, so then their attribution to external causes is impossible, and this answer does not suit the flow of the author's text because he mentions in the proof accidental matters. 'It is said, etc.': It was objected that this definition only applies to the efficient cause, and it was answered: The intended meaning is that the existence of another has a need for its existence in general, and it is better to define 'cause' as that which a thing needs in its existence so that it includes the privative cause as well, and the answer does not require the strain of reducing the privative cause to the existential cause. 'And they are four, etc.': It was said: The enumeration is refuted by condition, instrument, and absence of impediment, and it was answered: The thing being divided is the cause of a thing without mediation, and its divisions are four, and condition, instrument, and absence of impediment are not causes per se.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines 'cause' and its four types, while addressing common objections. The author begins by stating that a cause is something whose existence leads to the existence of another. He then enumerates the four causes: material, formal, efficient, and final. Each is illustrated with the example of a pot: clay (material), the shape (formal), the potter (efficient), and the purpose (final). The passage then discusses a problem: if the efficient cause is simple (non-composite), it cannot produce multiple effects or be permanent. This is a reference to the principle that a simple cause has a single effect. An answer tries to restrict the discussion to bodies stripped of external factors, but it is noted that this answer does not match the author's argument because his proof involves accidental factors. Next, an objection is raised that the given definition of cause only fits the efficient cause. The response clarifies that the definition intends that the existence of the effect depends on the cause in a general sense, and they suggest a better definition that includes privative causes (like lack of impediment). Another objection is that the enumeration of four causes is incomplete because it excludes condition, instrument, and absence of impediment. The answer explains that the division here is for the cause directly (without mediation), and those other factors are not causes per se. Throughout, the passage shows the precision of Aristotelian causal analysis within Islamic philosophy.

Key Terms
علة
علة مادية
علة صورية
علة فاعلية
علة غائية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which cause is described as being a part of the effect and necessitating its actual existence?
A) Material cause
B) Formal cause
C) Efficient cause
D) Final cause

Key Takeaway
The four causes — material, formal, efficient, final — provide a complete framework for analyzing why something exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the act of prayer (salat). Identify its material cause (the body's movements?), formal cause (the specific actions and recitations), efficient cause (the person praying), and final cause (obedience to God). Justify each.

Refining the Definition and Enumeration of Causes
Objections and responses to the definition and enumeration of causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objections against the definition and enumeration of causes, and explain how they are resolved.

Core Idea
The definition must be broad enough to cover all four causes; conditions and instruments are not direct causes.

After defining the four causes, the author anticipates two major objections: first, that the definition only applies to the efficient cause, and second, that the enumeration omits condition, instrument, and absence of impediment. Understanding these objections sharpens one's grasp of causality in Islamic philosophy.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the first objection
Find the objection that the definition of cause only fits the efficient cause. This is a challenge to the generality of the definition.
The objection is implied by 'قيل عليه'.
2
2. Understand the response to the first objection
The response clarifies that the definition intends a general dependence of the effect on the cause, not specific to efficient causality. They also suggest an alternative definition that includes privative causes.
3
3. Identify the second objection
The second objection is that the enumeration of four causes is incomplete because it leaves out condition, instrument, and absence of impediment.
4
4. Understand the response to the second objection
The answer is that the division is for the cause directly (without mediator), and those other factors are not causes per se but necessary conditions.

فصل في العلة والمعلول العلة تقال لكل ما له وجود في نفسه، ثم يحصل من وجوده وجود غيره، وهي أربعة أقسام مادية وصورية وفاعلية وغائية. أما المادية فهي التي تكون جزءا من المعلول، لكن لا يجب بها أن يكون موجودا بالفعل كالطين للكوز، وأما العلة الصورية فهي التي تكون جزءا من المعلول، لكن يجب بها أن يكون المعلول موجودا بالفعل كالصورة للكوز، وأما الفاعلية فهي التي يكون منها وجود المعلول كالفاعل للكوز، وأما الغائية فهي التي لأجلها وجود المعلول كالغرض المطلوب من الكوز. ثم العلة الفاعلية متى كانت بسيطة استحال دائمة وأكثرية. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه أن الكلام في الآثار التي تصدر عن الأجسام حين تجردها عن الأمور الخارجة، فلا يمكن حينئذ استنادها إلى أسباب خارجة، وهذا الجواب لا يلائم سوق كلام المصنف ؛ لذكره في الدليل أمورا اتفاقية. تقال إلخ : قيل عليه إن هذا التعريف لا يصدق إلا على العلة الفاعلية، وأجيب عنه: أن المراد أن يكون لوجود غيره حاجة إلى وجوده في الجملة والأولى أن تفسر العلة بما يحتاج إليه شيء في وجوده حتى يتناول العلة العدمية أيضا، ولا يحتاج في الجواب إلى التكلف بإرجاع العلة العدمية إلى العلة الوجودية وهي أربعة إلخ: قيل: الحصر منقوض بالشرط والمعد وعدم المانع، وأجيب عنه: أن المقسم هو علة الشيء بلا واسطة، وأقسامه أربعة والشرط والمعد وعدم المانع ليست علة بالذات.
Chapter on cause and effect. 'Cause' is said of everything that has existence in itself, then from its existence the existence of another arises. It has four divisions: material, formal, efficient, and final. As for the material cause, it is that which is a part of the effect, but it is not necessary that by it the effect exists in act, like clay for a pot. As for the formal cause, it is that which is a part of the effect, but it is necessary that by it the effect exists in act, like the shape for a pot. As for the efficient cause, it is that from which the existence of the effect comes, like the potter for a pot. As for the final cause, it is that for the sake of which the existence of the effect is, like the purpose sought from the pot. Then the efficient cause, when it is simple, cannot be perpetual or multiple. It can be answered that the discussion is about the effects that proceed from bodies when they are stripped of external matters, so then their attribution to external causes is impossible, and this answer does not suit the flow of the author's text because he mentions in the proof accidental matters. 'It is said, etc.': It was objected that this definition only applies to the efficient cause, and it was answered: The intended meaning is that the existence of another has a need for its existence in general, and it is better to define 'cause' as that which a thing needs in its existence so that it includes the privative cause as well, and the answer does not require the strain of reducing the privative cause to the existential cause. 'And they are four, etc.': It was said: The enumeration is refuted by condition, instrument, and absence of impediment, and it was answered: The thing being divided is the cause of a thing without mediation, and its divisions are four, and condition, instrument, and absence of impediment are not causes per se.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage defines 'cause' and its four types, while addressing common objections. The author begins by stating that a cause is something whose existence leads to the existence of another. He then enumerates the four causes: material, formal, efficient, and final. Each is illustrated with the example of a pot: clay (material), the shape (formal), the potter (efficient), and the purpose (final). The passage then discusses a problem: if the efficient cause is simple (non-composite), it cannot produce multiple effects or be permanent. This is a reference to the principle that a simple cause has a single effect. An answer tries to restrict the discussion to bodies stripped of external factors, but it is noted that this answer does not match the author's argument because his proof involves accidental factors. Next, an objection is raised that the given definition of cause only fits the efficient cause. The response clarifies that the definition intends that the existence of the effect depends on the cause in a general sense, and they suggest a better definition that includes privative causes (like lack of impediment). Another objection is that the enumeration of four causes is incomplete because it excludes condition, instrument, and absence of impediment. The answer explains that the division here is for the cause directly (without mediation), and those other factors are not causes per se. Throughout, the passage shows the precision of Aristotelian causal analysis within Islamic philosophy.

The Complete Cause and the Necessity of the Effect
Defines complete cause and proves necessity of effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the complete cause, reconstruct the proof that its realization necessitates the effect, and distinguish between necessary through another (wajib bi'l-ghayr) and possible in itself (mumkin bi'l-dhat).

Core Idea
The complete cause necessitates the effect, making it necessary through another while remaining contingent in itself.

In this passage, the author defines the 'complete cause' (al-'illa al-tamma) and demonstrates a crucial causal principle: when the complete cause is realized, the effect must exist. This principle underpins Islamic philosophers' understanding of divine causality and the contingent nature of the world. We will walk through the definition and the proof, then examine a subtle objection regarding simple causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definition of al-'illa al-tamma in the Arabic text. It is described as 'the totality of matters considered in its existence when realized.' This is the complete cause.
The term appears at the beginning of the passage.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'the totality of matters' (jins) — a set of conditions or parts. The differentia is that this totality, when realized, makes the effect's existence necessary. So the complete cause is a set of conditions whose realization necessitates the effect.
The commentary adds that composition is inherent because the cause involves multiple matters.
3
Examine the proof of necessity
The author argues by contradiction: assume the effect is not necessary when the complete cause is realized. Then it would be either impossible (which is absurd since it exists) or possible (which would require an additional preponderator, contradicting the completeness of the cause). Hence the effect must be necessary upon realization of the complete cause.
This is a proof by elimination of the two other modal categories.
4
Distinguish necessary through another from possible in itself
The effect is necessary (wajib) due to its cause, not in itself. When considered apart from the cause, its essence is neutral to existence and non-existence — this is mumkin bi'l-dhat. The passage explicitly states: 'if we consider its quiddity in itself, existence and non-existence are not necessary for it.'
The necessity is accidental (bil-ghayr), not essential.
5
Consider the objection about simple causes
An objector says that for a simple cause like the First Cause (God), the definition of complete cause (involving multiple matters) does not apply. The response is that the effect's possibility must be considered, implying a composition in the causal relation (i.e., the cause plus the effect's possibility), so the definition holds.
This shows the robustness of the definition: even simple causes require consideration of the effect's possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عند تحقق جملة الأمور المعتبرة في تحققه؛ لأنه لو لم يكن واجب الوجود حينئذ، فإما أن يكون ممتنع الوجود، وهو محال وإلا لما وجد، أو ممكن الوجود، فيحتاج إلى مرجح يخرجه من القوة إلى الفعل، فلا يكون جملة الأمور المعتبرة في وجوده حاصلة، وقد فرضناها حاصلة، هذا خلف فبان أن المعلول يجب وجوده عند تحقق العلة التامة، فيكون واجبا لغيره ممكنا بالذات؛ لأنا لو اعتبرنا ماهيته من حيث هي هي لا يجب لها الوجود ولا العدم. هداية: كون الشيء موجودا لا ينافي تأثير العلة الفاعلية فيه، لأن الشيء عند تحقق إلخ قيل عليه إن هذا التفسير للعلة التامة لا يصدق إذا كانت بسيطة كالباري للعقل الأول عند الفلاسفة، وأجيب عنه أنه لا بد من اعتبار إمكان المعلول حتى يصح تأثير الفاعل، فالتركيب لازم. ممكن الوجود أي أن يكون وجود المعلول وعدمه متساويين لا ترجيح لأحدهما على الآخر حين وجود العلة التامة، فلا تكفي العلة التامة لترجيح الوجود على العدم، فاحتيج إلى مرجح آخر، فصار وجود المعلول موقوفا على مرجح آخر، فلا تكون جملة الأمور المعتبرة في وجوده متحققة فلاح أن ما فرضناه من قبل أنه علة تامة لم تكن علة تامة، وهذا خلف، فلما تبين أن وجود المعلول حين وجود العلة التامة ليس بممتنع ولا بممكن، أنه واجب؛ لانحصار أحوال المفهومات في ثلاثة وهذا الوجوب ليس ذاتيا بل غيريا، وإذا اعتبر المعلول من حيث هو هو مع قطع النظر عن علته فليس بضروري الوجود والعدم؛ لأن الواجب بالذات والممتنع بالذات كلاهما مستغنيان عن فيكون ممكن الوجود؛ لأن الإمكان عبارة عن عدم ضرورة الوجود والعدم.
When the totality of matters considered in its existence is realized, because if it were not necessary existence at that moment, then either it would be impossible existence – which is absurd, otherwise it would not exist – or possible existence, in which case it needs a preponderator to bring it from potentiality to actuality, so the totality of matters considered in its existence would not be realized, whereas we have assumed it realized; this is a contradiction. Thus it becomes clear that the effect must exist upon the realization of the complete cause, so it is necessary through another, possible in essence; because if we consider its quiddity in itself, existence and non-existence are not necessary for it. Guidance: That a thing exists does not negate the influence of the efficient cause upon it, because the thing upon realization etc. It was said against this that this interpretation of the complete cause does not hold if the cause is simple, like the First Cause for the First Intellect according to the philosophers. The answer was that it is necessary to consider the possibility of the effect for the influence of the agent to be valid, so composition is necessary. Possible existence, i.e., the existence and non-existence of the effect are equal, no preponderance of one over the other upon the existence of the complete cause, so the complete cause does not suffice to preponderate existence over non-existence, so another preponderator is needed, so the existence of the effect becomes dependent on another preponderator, so the totality of matters considered in its existence would not be realized; it becomes clear that what we assumed before to be a complete cause was not a complete cause, and this is a contradiction. So when it became clear that the existence of the effect upon the existence of the complete cause is neither impossible nor possible, that it is necessary; because the states of concepts are confined to three, and this necessity is not essential but from another. And if the effect is considered in itself, cut off from its cause, it is not necessary of existence nor non-existence, because the necessary in essence and the impossible in essence are both independent of [?], so it is possible existence, because possibility is the absence of necessity of existence and non-existence.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage defines the complete cause (al-'illa al-tamma) and demonstrates that the effect is necessary upon its realization, while remaining possible in itself. The author begins by presenting a proof: if the effect were not necessary when the complete cause is realized, it would either be impossible or possible. It cannot be impossible, for then it would not exist at all. If it were possible, it would need an external preponderator, contradicting the assumption that the complete cause is already realized. Thus the effect must be necessary. However, this necessity is not intrinsic but derived from the cause (wajib bi'l-ghayr), as the effect's essence considered alone is neutral to existence and non-existence (mumkin bi'l-dhat). The commentary then clarifies that existence does not negate causal influence, and addresses an objection: for a simple cause like the First Cause, the definition of complete cause might not apply because it has no parts. The response is that the effect's possibility must be considered, implying composition in the cause-effect relation. Finally, the passage expands on possibility: upon realization of the complete cause, the effect is neither impossible nor possible; it becomes necessary, but this necessity is accidental. Considered apart from its cause, it remains possible. This distinction is crucial for understanding contingency and divine causality. For example, the world considered in itself is possible, but when the complete cause (the Creator) is realized, it becomes necessary through Him.

The effect is necessary through its complete cause but remains contingent in itself; this distinction preserves both divine causality and the world's contingency.

The Five Types of Substance
Author defines substance and enumerates its five types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five types of substance and explain the logical basis for their division.

Core Idea
Substance is defined by a three-way division: locus, inherence, or neither.

The author defines substance not by a single genus but by enumerating what a substance can be: hyle (matter), form, body, soul, or intellect. This is a descriptive definition (rasm) through division, not a strict genus-differentia definition. Understanding this division is crucial for later discussions in kalam about the nature of the world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division point
The passage begins by asking if it is a locus (mahall) or an inherence (hulul). Recognize that these are two mutually exclusive possibilities.
2
If it is a locus, it is hyle (al-hayula)
Hyle is the receptive matter.
3
If it is an inherence, it is form (al-surah)
Form is the actualizing principle in matter.
4
If neither, consider composition
If it is composed of hyle and form, it is body (jism).
5
If not composite, consider relation to bodies
If it manages a body, it is soul (nafs); otherwise, it is intellect (aql).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان محلا فهو الهيولى، وإن كان حالا فهو الصورة، وإن لم يكن حالا ولا محلا، فإن كان مركبا منهما فهو الجسم، وإن لم يكن كذلك فإن كان متعلقا بالأجسام تعلق التدبير والتصرف فهو النفس، وإلا فهو العقل، والجوهر ليس جنسا لهذه الأقسام الخمسة؛ إذ لو كان جنسا لكان ما يدخل تحته مركبا من جنس وفصل، وليس كذلك؛ لأن النفس ليست مركبة منهما؛ لأنها تعقل الماهية البسيطة فلا تكون مركبة، وإلا لزم انقسام الماهية البسيطة الحالة فيها، هذا خلف.
If it is a locus, it is hyle; if it is an inherence, it is form; if it is neither a locus nor an inherence, then if it is composed of them, it is body; if not, then if it is related to bodies with a relation of management and disposition, it is soul; otherwise, it is intellect. Substance is not a genus for these five divisions; because if it were a genus, what falls under it would be composed of genus and differentia, but that is not the case, because the soul is not composed of them, for it apprehends the simple quiddity and thus is not composite; otherwise, the division of the simple quiddity inhering in it would follow, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage defines substance (jawhar) by dividing it into five types based on ontological relations: hyle (matter), form, body, soul, and intellect. The definition is not a standard genus-differentia definition; rather, it is a rasm (description) through division. The author then argues that substance is not a genus for these five, because if it were, each type would have to be composed of genus and differentia, but the soul, for example, is simple (basita) and not composite. The argument uses an absurdity (khulf): if the soul were composite, its simple quiddity would be divided, which is impossible. This demonstrates that the five are not species under a single genus, but rather distinct types of substance.

Key Takeaway

Why Substance Is Not a Genus
Author argues that substance is not a genus for its divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the author's proof that substance is not a genus for its five types.

Core Idea
If substance were a genus, each type would be composite; but soul is simple, so substance cannot be a genus.

After listing the five types, the author argues that they are not species of a common genus. The proof is a reductio: assume substance is a genus, then each type would have genus+differentia composition. But the soul, one of the types, is a simple quiddity and cannot be composite. Therefore, the assumption is false.

Guided Path
1
State the hypothesis
The author says: 'if substance were a genus for these five divisions, then what falls under it would be composed of genus and differentia.'
2
Identify the counterexample
He takes the soul as a case. Soul is a substance type. If substance were a genus, soul would be composed of genus (substance) and differentia.
3
Prove soul is not composite
Soul apprehends simple quiddity, so its quiddity is simple; if it were composite, the simple quiddity would be divided, which is absurd (khulf).
4
Conclusion
Therefore, the hypothesis that substance is a genus leads to absurdity; so substance is not a genus.

Source Text

The Proof that Existence is Essence for the Necessary Being
Author argues that existence is not accidental but identical to essence for the Necessary Being

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the philosophers' conditional reductio proof and identify the meaning of 'wujud' at stake.

Core Idea
Existence is not an accident added to God; it is His very reality.

This passage tackles a foundational debate in Islamic metaphysics: is God's existence something additional to His essence, or is it identical? The philosophers (hukama) argue for identity using a logical proof that reduces the additional view to absurdity. This lesson walks you through their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the three meanings of 'wujud'
The passage identifies three senses: the infinitive concept, the verbal noun 'being', and the real existence that is the source of effects. Only the third is the subject of the debate.
The philosophers equate this real existence with the essence of the Necessary.
2
Identify the thesis and antithesis
The philosophers claim existence is identical to essence; the theologians claim it is additional. The argument targets the additional view.
3
Parse the conditional chain
The argument assumes existence is additional. From that, it deduces: (a) existence would be an accident, (b) accidents are possible and need a cause, (c) the cause must be either the essence itself or something else.
4
Follow the reductio
If the cause is the essence itself, then the essence must exist before the accidental existence, but that accidental existence is the essence's own existence — leading to self-priority, which is absurd. If the cause is something else, an infinite regress follows (implied).
5
Conclude the proof
Since both branches lead to contradiction, the initial assumption (existence is additional) is false. Therefore, existence is identical to essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن وجود واجب الوجود نفس حقيقته لأن وجوده لو كان زائدا على حقيقته لكان عارضا لها، ولو كان عارضا الوجود اعلم أن الوجود قد يطلق ويراد به المعنى المصدري البديهي التصور، ويراد به الكون المصدري، ومعناه بالفارسية: هستن و بودن، وقد يطلق ويراد به منشأ اختراع هذا المعنى المصدري ومنبعه وهو مناط الموجودية وترتب الأحكام والآثار، وهو الوجود الأصلي والحقيقي، فاختلف الحكماء والمتكلمون في ذلك المنشأ، فالحكماء قالوا: إنه عين ذاته والمتكلمون حكموا بأنه زائد على ذاته، وأما المعنى المصدري فلا يصلح أن يكون عين حقيقة من الحقائق، فضلا أن يكون عين الواجب، استدل الحكماء على قولهم بأنه لو كان زائدا على ذاته لكان عارضا لحقيقته؛ لامتناع كونه جزءا، ولو كان عارضا لها لكان ممكنا لذاته؛ لأن كل عارض محتاج في وجوده إلى محل يقوم به، وكل محتاج في وجوده إلى الغير ممكن، والممكن لا بد له من جاعل ومؤثر يوجده ويخرجه من العدم، فذلك المؤثر إن كان من نفس تلك الحقيقة الواجبة لذاتها يلزم أن تكون موجودة قبل وجودها العارض لها الممكن المحتاج إليها؛ لأن العلة الموجدة للشيء يجب تقدمها على معلولها بالوجود فيلزم أن تكون تلك الحقيقة الواجبة لذاتها موجودة قبل وجودها؛ لأن الوجود العارض المعلول المتأخر هو وجودها المتقدم بالعلية فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه هذا خلف، وإن كان غير الحقيقة
Chapter on that the existence of the Necessary of existence is identical to His essence. Because if His existence were additional to His essence, it would be an accident to it, and if it were an accident... Know that 'existence' (al-wujud) is sometimes used to mean the infinitive meaning, which is self-evident in conception, and sometimes used to mean the infinitive 'to be', its meaning in Persian: 'hasten' and 'budan'. And sometimes it is used to mean the source of the invention of this infinitive meaning and its origin, which is the basis of 'existent-ness' and the regulation of judgments and effects, and that is the original and real existence. Thus the philosophers (hukama) and the theologians (mutakallimun) differed concerning that origin: the philosophers said it is identical to His essence, and the theologians ruled that it is additional to His essence. As for the infinitive meaning, it cannot be identical to the reality of any reality, let alone the identity of the Necessary. The philosophers argued for their statement: if it were additional to His essence, it would be an accident to His reality (since it cannot be a part). And if it were an accident to it, it would be possible in itself, because every accident is needful in its existence of a substrate in which it subsists, and everything needful in its existence to another is possible. And the possible must have a maker and effector that brings it into existence and brings it out of non-existence. That effector, if it is from the same necessary reality itself, then it would follow that that reality exists before its accidental existence which is possible and needful of it, because the efficient cause of a thing must precede its effect in existence. Thus that necessary reality would exist before its existence, because the accidental existence, which is the effect posterior, is its existence prior by causality, so the priority of a thing over itself would follow – this is a vicious circle. And if it is other than the reality...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophical position that for the Necessary Being, existence is identical to essence. It begins by distinguishing three meanings of 'existence': the infinitive concept (self-evident), the verbal noun ('being'), and the real existence which is the source of effects and judgments. The philosophers (hukama) affirm that this real existence is identical to the essence of the Necessary, while the theologians (mutakallimun) say it is additional. The proof: if existence were additional, it would be an accident. Every accident needs a substrate and is therefore possible (since it depends on another). A possible needs a cause. That cause must either be the essence itself or something else. If it is the essence itself, then the essence must exist before the accidental existence that is its own existence, leading to a priority of self over self, which is absurd. If the cause is something else, an infinite regress might follow (the text breaks off but implies a similar problem). Thus existence cannot be accidental and must be essential. This is a standard Peripatetic argument against the Ash'ari view that God's existence is an attribute distinct from His essence.

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud): The real existence which is the basis of being and effects; in this context, the philosophers consider it identical to the essence of the Necessary. [Note: In later Sufi thought, al-wujud may mean absolute existence; here it is a technical term in ontology.]
الحقيقة (al-haqiqa): The essence or reality of a thing; for the Necessary, it is that which does not require a cause and is self-sufficient. [Note: No significant shift.]
العارض (al-‘arid): An accident that inheres in a subject; it is possible because it depends on the subject for existence. [Note: In logic, 'arid often means a non-essential predicate; here it is metaphysical.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, why can existence not be an accident of the Necessary Being?
A) Because the Necessary Being is simple and cannot have accidents.
B) Because if existence were accidental, it would need a cause, leading to circularity or regress.
C) Because accidents are only in material substances.
D) Because existence is a universal concept applying to all things.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers use a conditional reductio to prove that for the Necessary Being, existence is not an accident but identical to essence, because any added existence would require a cause leading to absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the theologians' position (existence is additional) and try to construct a counter-argument that avoids the absurdity. Where might the philosophers' reasoning be challenged? Write one objection a mutakallim could raise.
?
Identify another theological debate where a similar reductio argument is used (e.g., the debate on the eternity of the world) and outline the conditional chain.

The Necessary Existent's Self-Knowledge: A Proof from Immateriality
Author proves the Necessary Existent's self-knowledge through immateriality and presence of essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism proving the Necessary Existent's self-knowledge and explain how the definition of knowledge supports the conclusion.

Core Idea
Self-knowledge follows from immateriality because knowledge is the presence of reality abstracted from matter.

This passage from Hidayat al-Hikma presents a concise proof that God, the Necessary Existent, knows Himself. The proof rests on two pillars: (1) the definition of knowledge as the presence of a thing's reality abstracted from matter, and (2) the claim that the Necessary Existent is immaterial. We will walk through the logical structure of this argument and see how it establishes divine self-knowledge without introducing any dualism in the divine essence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion
Find the statement that the author is trying to prove. It is: 'the Necessary Existent knows itself'. Mark this as the conclusion.
The conclusion is explicitly stated at the beginning: 'الواجب لذاته عالم بذاته'.
2
Locate the major premise
Find the general statement that links immateriality to self-knowledge. It is: 'Every immaterial being that perceives knows itself.' This is the major premise.
The author says: 'وكل مجرد عن المادة مدرك فهو عالم بذاته'.
3
Locate the minor premise
Find the statement that the Necessary Existent is immaterial. It is given as: 'لأنه مجرد عن المادة'.
Immateriality is asserted as a property of the Necessary Existent.
4
Extract the definition of knowledge
The author defines knowledge as 'the attainment of the reality of a thing, abstracted from matter and its accidents, by the perceiver.' Write down this definition.
The definition is: 'العلم هو حصول حقيقة الشيء مجردة عن المادة ولواحقها عند المدرك'.
5
Verify the conclusion using the definition
If the Necessary Existent is immaterial and its essence is present to itself (since it is self-subsistent), then by the definition, it has knowledge of itself. Check that the conditions of the definition are satisfied.
The author says: 'لأن ذاته حاصلة عنده فيكون عالما بذاته'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب لذاته عالم بذاته لأنه مجرد عن المادة، وكل مجرد عن المادة مدرك فهو عالم بذاته؛ لأن ذاته حاصلة عنده فيكون عالما بذاته؛ لأن العلم هو حصول حقيقة الشيء مجردة عن المادة ولواحقها عند المدرك، فالباري عالم بذاته. لمجود إلخ لأنه لو كان ماديا لكان حادثا؛ لأن كل ما يتعلق وجوده بها فهو حادث، ولو كان ماديا بمعنى المركب من الهيولى والصورة لكان أيضا حادثا؛ لتقدم الجزء على الكل. كل مجرد المراد به القائم بذاته؛ لئلا ينتقض بالصورة المعقولة، فإنها أنها ليست بعالمة لأن العلم حاصله أن العلم هو حصول المعلوم المجرد مجردة عن المادة وعوارضها عند الذات المجردة القائمة بنفسها، وهذه الذات فلما كان وجودها عندها حاصلا، فتكون عالمة بنفسها؛ لتحقق مناط المعلومية. اعلم أن علم الأشياء بالعقل يسمى تعقلا، وعلم الأشياء بالحواس يسمى إحساسا.
Chapter on that the Necessary Existent is knowing of itself because it is immaterial, and every immaterial thing that perceives is knowing of itself; because its essence is present to it, so it is knowing of itself; because knowledge is the attainment of the reality of a thing, abstracted from matter and its accidents, by the perceiver, so the Creator is knowing of itself. For the existent etc., because if it were material it would be temporally originated; for everything whose existence is connected to matter is temporally originated, and if it were material in the sense of composed of hyle and form, it would also be temporally originated, due to the priority of the part to the whole. By 'immaterial' is meant that which subsists by itself, so as not to be refuted by the intelligible form, for it is not knowing because knowledge is the attainment of the known abstracted, abstracted from matter and its accidents, by the immaterial essence subsisting by itself, and this essence, since its existence is present to it, is knowing of itself, due to the fulfillment of the condition of being known. Know that knowledge of things through the intellect is called intellection, and knowledge of things through the senses is called sensation.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage contains a proof that the Necessary Existent (God) knows itself. The major premise is that every immaterial being that perceives (i.e., has awareness) knows itself because its essence is present to it. The minor premise is that the Necessary Existent is immaterial. Therefore, it knows itself. The author defines knowledge as the presence of the reality of a thing, abstracted from matter, to the knower. An explanatory note clarifies that if the Necessary Existent were material, it would be temporally originated (hadith). It distinguishes between intellectual knowledge (ta'aqqul) and sensory knowledge (ihsas). The note emphasizes that immaterial means 'subsisting by itself' to avoid confusion with intelligible forms which are not knowing.

Key Terms
مجرد عن المادة
العلم
تعقل
إحساس

C) Because it is composite
D) Because it is a cause

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Existent knows itself because it is immaterial; immateriality makes the essence present to itself, fulfilling the definition of knowledge as the attainment of reality abstracted from matter.

Defining Divine Will (Irada)
Author defines divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of divine will (irada) and explain its logical conditions.

Core Idea
Divine will is God's approval of what emanates from His perfection.

The author begins by establishing that God is willing (mureed) of things. In this lesson, we dissect the precise definition of divine will: it is the attribute by which God, knowing what is good and compatible with His essence, causes it to emanate from His perfection. This definition distinguishes divine will from human will, which involves desire and need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is الإرادة (al-irada). Locate it in the Arabic: the phrase 'وهذا هو الإرادة' marks the definition.
2
Extract the conditions
The definition lists three conditions for something to be willed: (1) it is known to God (معلوم عند المبدأ), (2) it is good (خير), (3) it is not incompatible with His essence (غير مناف لماهيته).
These conditions ensure that will is not arbitrary.
3
Understand the causal flow
The thing emanates (فائض) from God's essence and perfection, and it is pleasing to Him (مرضي له). This emanation is the actualization of will.
4
State the definition
Combine the elements: divine will is the attribute by which God, knowing the good and compatible, brings it forth from His perfection, and it is pleasing to Him.
This is a hadd haqiqi (real definition) because it gives the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب مريد للأشياء وجواد. أما إرادته فلأن كل ما هو معلوم عند المبدأ وهو خير، غير مناف لماهيته فائض عن ذات المبدأ وكماله المقتضي لفيضانه، فذلك الشيء مرضي له، وهذا هو الإرادة. وأما جوده فنقول : الواجب لذاته إما أن يفعل بقصد وشوق إلى كمال أو يفعل لأنه نظام الخير في الوجود، فيوجد الأشياء على ما ينبغي لا لغرض وشوق، والأول محال؛ لما بينا أن واجب الوجود ليس له كمال منتظر، والقسم الثاني حق فهو الجواد. وهو خير وإنما قال: "وهو خير"؛ لأن الوجود خير كله ليس فيه شر أصلا، وإنما الشر عارض له بالقياس إلى شيء آخر، أما الوجود فهو في نفسه خير محض. فإن قيل في الوجود بعض الأشياء خير وبعضها شر والخير والشر كله صادر عنه تعالى؛ إذ لا خالق سواه، فكيف يصح قوله: "وهو خير؟ قلنا: الوجود الذي يقال له: "شر" له اعتباران اعتبار أنه موجود، وبهذا الاعتبار ليس بشر، بل هو خير؛ لأنه يترتب عليه كماله الذي أودع في وجوده واعتبار أنه ضار لشيء آخر، وبهذا الاعتبار يقال له : شر، فالشر إضافة عارضة له بالقياس إلى شيء آخر، والصادر عن الواجب سبحانه من حيث إنه موجود وبهذا الاعتبار ليس شرا حتى يمتنع صدوره عنه، وبهذا البيان ارتفع شبهة الثنوية التي قالت بالهين؛ بناء على زعمها أن في العالم خيرا وشرا ولا يمكن استنادهما إلى إله واحد ؛ لأن خالق الخير خير، وخالق الشر شر، والذات الواحدة لا تكون خيرا وشرا، فخالق الخير عند هذه الطائفة الضالة يزدان، وخالق الشر عندها "أهر من"، وعند أهل الإسلام خالق الخير والشر هو الله سبحانه، وقد علمت وجه التفصي عن الإشكال.
Chapter that the Necessary Existent is willing of things and generous. As for His will, because everything that is known to the First Principle and is good, not incompatible with His essence, emanates from the essence of the First Principle and His perfection which necessitates its emanation, so that thing is pleasing to Him, and this is will. As for His generosity, we say: The Necessary Existent either acts with purpose and longing for perfection, or He acts because it is the order of good in existence, so He brings things into existence as they ought, not for a purpose or longing. The first is impossible because we have shown that the Necessary Existent has no awaited perfection. The second division is true, so He is the Generous. And He is good. And He only said "He is good" because existence is entirely good; there is no evil in it at all. Evil is only accidental to it in relation to something else. As for existence, it is in itself pure good. If it is said: In existence some things are good and some are evil, and good and evil all emanate from Him the Exalted, since there is no creator besides Him, so how is His statement "He is good" valid? We reply: The existence that is called "evil" has two aspects: an aspect in that it exists, and in this aspect it is not evil, rather it is good because its perfection which is placed in its existence is arranged upon it; and an aspect in that it is harmful to something else, and in this aspect it is called evil. So evil is an accidental relation to it in relation to something else. What emanates from the Necessary Exalted is, insofar as it exists, and in this aspect it is not evil such that its emanation from Him would be impossible. And by this explanation, the doubt of the dualists who say there are two gods is removed, based on their claim that in the world there is good and evil, and they cannot be attributed to one God, because the creator of good is good and the creator of evil is evil, and one essence cannot be both good and evil. So the creator of good according to this misguided sect is Yazdan, and the creator of evil according to them is Ahriman. According to the people of Islam, the creator of good and evil is Allah the Exalted. And you have learned the way of extrication from the problem.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines two divine attributes: will (إرادة) and generosity (جود). The author defines will as the attribute by which everything known to God that is good and not incompatible with His essence emanates from Him, and that thing is pleasing to Him. For generosity, a disjunctive syllogism is used: God either acts with purpose and longing for perfection (which is impossible because He has no lacking perfection), or He acts because it is the order of good in existence, bringing things into existence as they should be without any goal or longing. The latter is true, so God is generous (الجواد). Then the author explains that God is good, because existence itself is pure good; evil is only accidental in relation to something else. A possible objection is raised: if evil exists, how can God be good? The reply is that what we call 'evil' has two aspects: as existent it is good (because it contains its own perfection), and as harmful to something else it is called evil. Thus evil is a relative relation, not absolute. This argument refutes the dualist claim that good and evil must come from two different gods (Yazdan and Ahriman). The author concludes that according to Islam, God is the creator of both good and evil, and the dilemma is resolved by this distinction.

Key Terms
إرادة
مبدأ
خير
إرادة
فائض
مرضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for something to be willed by God?
A) It is known to God.
B) It is good.
C) It is compatible with God's essence.
D) It is created by human effort.

Key Takeaway
Divine will is the attribute by which God brings into existence what is known, good, and compatible, emanating from His perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Using the same logical structure, identify the definition of divine knowledge (‘ilm) from the condition that God knows all things. What would the genus and differentia be? (Hint: Use the same pattern of knownness, compatibility, and emanation).

Proving God's Generosity with a Disjunctive Syllogism
Author defines generosity using a disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive syllogism used to prove God's generosity and verify its validity.

Core Idea
God is generous because He acts without need, bringing existence as it ought.

The author now proves that God is generous (jawad) using a disjunctive argument. He presents two mutually exclusive possibilities for why God acts, eliminates the one that implies need, and concludes the other. This lesson walks you through the logical steps and formalizes the argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction
The author states two options for God's action: (1) He acts with purpose and longing for perfection (بقصد وشوق إلى كمال), or (2) He acts because it is the order of good (نظام الخير) and brings things into existence as they ought, without purpose or longing.
The disjunction is exclusive and exhaustive: these are the only two possibilities.
2
Eliminate the first option
The first option is impossible because God has no awaited perfection (لا كمال منتظر). He is already perfect; thus acting for a purpose would imply a lack.
The author references a previous proof that the Necessary Existent has no perfection it awaits.
3
Affirm the second option
Since the first is false, the second must be true: God acts not for any purpose but because it is the order of good, and He brings things into existence as they ought to be.
4
Conclude the definition of generosity
This second option is the definition of generosity (الجود): giving what is fitting without need or compensation. Therefore, God is the Generous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting Dualism: Evil as Relational, Not Absolute
Author explains nature of evil and refutes dualism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the author's refutation of dualism by distinguishing evil as a relational accident and existence as good.

Core Idea
Evil is not a positive creation but a relative relation; existence itself is pure good.

After establishing God's generosity, the author addresses a major objection: if God is good, how can evil exist? He also refutes the dualist claim that good and evil must come from different gods. This lesson unpacks the analysis of evil as relational and shows how it preserves monotheism.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector says: some things in existence are good and some are evil, and both come from God. How can God be called good if He creates evil?
This objection challenges God's absolute goodness.
2
Analyze the two aspects of a thing called evil
The author says: a thing that is called 'evil' has two aspects. (1) As existing, it is good because it has its own perfection. (2) As harmful to something else, it is called evil. Therefore, evil is a relation (إضافة عارضة).
The existence itself is always good; the evil is accidental.
3
Apply to divine creation
What emanates from God, insofar as it exists, is good. So there is no positive evil emanating from God. Evil is only in relation to something else, and that relation does not prevent the thing's existence from being good.
4
Refute dualism
Dualists claim that good and evil require two creators (Yazdan and Ahriman). But since evil is merely a relation, not a positive being, there is no need for a second creator. One God creates all existence, and the relations of harm arise from interactions, not from a separate source of evil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في أن الواجب مريد للأشياء وجواد. أما إرادته فلأن كل ما هو معلوم عند المبدأ وهو خير، غير مناف لماهيته فائض عن ذات المبدأ وكماله المقتضي لفيضانه، فذلك الشيء مرضي له، وهذا هو الإرادة. وأما جوده فنقول : الواجب لذاته إما أن يفعل بقصد وشوق إلى كمال أو يفعل لأنه نظام الخير في الوجود، فيوجد الأشياء على ما ينبغي لا لغرض وشوق، والأول محال؛ لما بينا أن واجب الوجود ليس له كمال منتظر، والقسم الثاني حق فهو الجواد. وهو خير وإنما قال: "وهو خير"؛ لأن الوجود خير كله ليس فيه شر أصلا، وإنما الشر عارض له بالقياس إلى شيء آخر، أما الوجود فهو في نفسه خير محض. فإن قيل في الوجود بعض الأشياء خير وبعضها شر والخير والشر كله صادر عنه تعالى؛ إذ لا خالق سواه، فكيف يصح قوله: "وهو خير؟ قلنا: الوجود الذي يقال له: "شر" له اعتباران اعتبار أنه موجود، وبهذا الاعتبار ليس بشر، بل هو خير؛ لأنه يترتب عليه كماله الذي أودع في وجوده واعتبار أنه ضار لشيء آخر، وبهذا الاعتبار يقال له : شر، فالشر إضافة عارضة له بالقياس إلى شيء آخر، والصادر عن الواجب سبحانه من حيث إنه موجود وبهذا الاعتبار ليس شرا حتى يمتنع صدوره عنه، وبهذا البيان ارتفع شبهة الثنوية التي قالت بالهين؛ بناء على زعمها أن في العالم خيرا وشرا ولا يمكن استنادهما إلى إله واحد ؛ لأن خالق الخير خير، وخالق الشر شر، والذات الواحدة لا تكون خيرا وشرا، فخالق الخير عند هذه الطائفة الضالة يزدان، وخالق الشر عندها "أهر من"، وعند أهل الإسلام خالق الخير والشر هو الله سبحانه، وقد علمت وجه التفصي عن الإشكال.
Chapter that the Necessary Existent is willing of things and generous. As for His will, because everything that is known to the First Principle and is good, not incompatible with His essence, emanates from the essence of the First Principle and His perfection which necessitates its emanation, so that thing is pleasing to Him, and this is will. As for His generosity, we say: The Necessary Existent either acts with purpose and longing for perfection, or He acts because it is the order of good in existence, so He brings things into existence as they ought, not for a purpose or longing. The first is impossible because we have shown that the Necessary Existent has no awaited perfection. The second division is true, so He is the Generous. And He is good. And He only said "He is good" because existence is entirely good; there is no evil in it at all. Evil is only accidental to it in relation to something else. As for existence, it is in itself pure good. If it is said: In existence some things are good and some are evil, and good and evil all emanate from Him the Exalted, since there is no creator besides Him, so how is His statement "He is good" valid? We reply: The existence that is called "evil" has two aspects: an aspect in that it exists, and in this aspect it is not evil, rather it is good because its perfection which is placed in its existence is arranged upon it; and an aspect in that it is harmful to something else, and in this aspect it is called evil. So evil is an accidental relation to it in relation to something else. What emanates from the Necessary Exalted is, insofar as it exists, and in this aspect it is not evil such that its emanation from Him would be impossible. And by this explanation, the doubt of the dualists who say there are two gods is removed, based on their claim that in the world there is good and evil, and they cannot be attributed to one God, because the creator of good is good and the creator of evil is evil, and one essence cannot be both good and evil. So the creator of good according to this misguided sect is Yazdan, and the creator of evil according to them is Ahriman. According to the people of Islam, the creator of good and evil is Allah the Exalted. And you have learned the way of extrication from the problem.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines two divine attributes: will (إرادة) and generosity (جود). The author defines will as the attribute by which everything known to God that is good and not incompatible with His essence emanates from Him, and that thing is pleasing to Him. For generosity, a disjunctive syllogism is used: God either acts with purpose and longing for perfection (which is impossible because He has no lacking perfection), or He acts because it is the order of good in existence, bringing things into existence as they should be without any goal or longing. The latter is true, so God is generous (الجواد). Then the author explains that God is good, because existence itself is pure good; evil is only accidental in relation to something else. A possible objection is raised: if evil exists, how can God be good? The reply is that what we call 'evil' has two aspects: as existent it is good (because it contains its own perfection), and as harmful to something else it is called evil. Thus evil is a relative relation, not absolute. This argument refutes the dualist claim that good and evil must come from two different gods (Yazdan and Ahriman). The author concludes that according to Islam, God is the creator of both good and evil, and the dilemma is resolved by this distinction.

The First Intellect's Dual Quiddity and the Origin of Multiplicity
Author explains how multiplicity arises from the first intellect's dual nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the first intellect's quiddity allows it to produce two distinct effects while preserving the principle that from the One only one proceeds.

Core Idea
The One's first effect is one, but its intrinsic duality permits multiplicity.

In this passage, the author tackles a key problem in Avicennan cosmology: if God is absolutely simple, how can He create a manifold universe? The solution lies in the nature of the first created intellect, which possesses both necessity from its cause and possibility in itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle
The principle 'from the One only one proceeds' (al-wāḥid lā yaṣduru ʿanhu illā al-wāḥid) implies that God's first effect must be one. Locate this principle in the Arabic text.
2
Examine the first intellect's quiddity
The first intellect has a quiddity (māhiyya) that is possible in itself (mumkin li-dhātih) and necessary by its cause (wājib bi-al-ghayr). This gives it two metaphysical aspects.
This duality is real, not a mere mental distinction, according to Avicenna.
3
Connect aspects to emanations
The nobler aspect (necessity-by-another) is the principle for the second intellect; the lesser aspect (possibility-in-itself) is the principle for the outermost sphere. Understand why the nobler effect follows the nobler aspect.
4
Verify preservation of the principle
The first intellect is one entity with two aspects, so it produces two effects without violating 'from the One only one proceeds' because the effects are from different aspects, not from separate principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد مر أن واجب الوجود واحد، ومعلوله الأول هو العقل المحض والأفلاك معلولات للعقول، لكن الأفلاك فيها كثرة فيكون مبادئها كثيرة؛ لما بينا أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه الا الواحد، والعقل الذي يصدر عنه الفلك الأعظم فيه كثرة، لكن لا باعتبار صدوره عن واجب الوجود، بل باعتبار أن له ماهية ممكنة الوجود لذاتها واجبة الوجود لعلتها، فيلزمه وجوب الوجود بالغير وإمكان الوجود لذاته، فيكون بأحد هذين الاعتبارين مبدأ للعقل الثاني، وبالاعتبار الآخر مبدأ للفلك الأعظم والمعلول الأشرف يجب أن يكون تابعا للجهة التي هي أشرف الجهات في العقل، فيكون بما هو موجود واجب الوجود بالغير مبدأ للعقل الثاني، وبما هو موجود ممكن الوجود لذاته مبدأ للفلك الأعظم، وبهذا الطريق يصدر عن كل عقل عقل وفلك، وكذلك إلى العقل التاسع، فيصدر عنه فلك القمر الذي يلي كرة النار، وعقل عاشر، ولعلهم إنما اكتفوا على العاشر؛ لأن الدليل على وجود العقول كثرة الأفلاك، والأفلاك تسعة، والصادر الأول عن الباري تعالى عقل محض ليس بإزائه فلك، ثم يصدر عن العقل الأول عقل وفلك، وهكذا إلى العقل التاسع، فصار عدد العقول عشرة، وعدد الأفلاك تسعة.
It has passed that the Necessary Existent is One, and His first effect is the pure intellect, and the spheres are effects of the intellects, but the spheres have multiplicity, so their principles are many. Since we have explained that from the One only one proceeds, the intellect from which the greatest sphere proceeds has multiplicity, but not by virtue of its emanation from the Necessary Existent, but by virtue of its having a quiddity that is possible in itself and necessary by its cause. Therefore, necessity by another and possibility for itself are entailed for it. So by one of these two aspects it is a principle for the second intellect, and by the other aspect it is a principle for the greatest sphere. The nobler effect must follow the nobler aspect in the intellect. So, by virtue of its being existent and necessary by another, it is a principle for the second intellect, and by virtue of its being existent and possible in itself, it is a principle for the greatest sphere. In this way, from every intellect an intellect and a sphere proceed, and so on up to the ninth intellect, from which proceeds the sphere of the moon that follows the sphere of fire, and a tenth intellect. Perhaps they only stopped at the tenth because the evidence for the existence of intellects is the multiplicity of spheres, and the spheres are nine. The first emanation from the Exalted Creator is a pure intellect with no sphere opposite it. Then from the first intellect proceeds an intellect and a sphere, and so on up to the ninth intellect, so the number of intellects becomes ten and the number of spheres nine.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the Neoplatonic emanation scheme adopted by Avicenna and his followers. The author begins by recalling that the Necessary Existent is One, and from One only one proceeds. The first effect is a pure intellect (al-ʿaql al-maḥḍ). This intellect, though emanating from the One, contains multiplicity because it has a quiddity (māhiyya) that is possible in itself (mumkin al-wujūd li-dhātihā) and necessary by its cause (wājib al-wujūd li-ʿillatihā). This duality gives the intellect two aspects: one of necessity-by-another (wujūb bi’l-ghayr) and one of possibility-in-itself (imkān al-wujūd li-dhātihi). By the nobler aspect (necessity-by-another), it emanates a second intellect; by the lesser aspect (possibility), it emanates the outermost sphere (al-falak al-aʿẓam). This process continues: each intellect emanates a lower intellect and a sphere, until the ninth intellect emanates the sphere of the moon and a tenth intellect (the Active Intellect). The author notes that the proof for the existence of ten intellects is the fact that there are nine spheres, and since the first effect (the First Intellect) has no sphere, the total intellects are ten. This explanation preserves the principle 'from the One only one proceeds' while accounting for cosmic multiplicity. The argument is a compound of causal reasoning and metaphysical deduction about quiddity and existence.

Key Terms
الماهية الممكنة
وجوب بالغير
العقل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Necessity-by-another and possibility-in-itself
C) Materiality and spirituality
D) Actuality and potentiality

Key Takeaway
The first intellect's dual quiddity enables the first multiplicity in the universe while preserving the principle that the One creates only one thing directly.

The Emanation Cascade: Ten Intellects and Nine Spheres
Author explains the full emanation sequence from first intellect to tenth, and the correspondence between intellects and spheres.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the complete emanation sequence from the first intellect to the tenth intellect and verify that the number of intellects (10) corresponds to the number of spheres (9) plus the first intellect.

Core Idea
The nine spheres arise from nine intellects, making ten intellects in total.

After establishing how the first intellect produces two effects, the author extends the pattern to the entire cosmos. Each subsequent intellect replicates the same structure, generating a lower intellect and a sphere, until we reach the ninth intellect, which gives the sphere of the moon and the tenth (active) intellect.

Guided Path
1
Note the pattern
The passage states: 'from every intellect an intellect and a sphere proceed.' This pattern is repeated for each intellect. Recognize that this is a recursive emanation.
2
Identify the starting point
The first intellect (pure intellect) has no sphere; from it proceeds the second intellect and the outermost sphere (al-falak al-aʿẓam).
3
Continue the pattern
From the second intellect comes the third intellect and the next sphere, and so on up to the ninth intellect. Each step produces one lower intellect and one lower sphere.
4
Observe the final step
From the ninth intellect emanates the sphere of the moon (the lowest sphere) and the tenth intellect (the Active Intellect). The tenth intellect has no sphere.
5
Count and reconcile
Count the intellects: first (no sphere) + second through ninth (each with a sphere) + tenth (no sphere) = ten. Spheres: nine. Verify the author's statement: 'the number of intellects becomes ten and the number of spheres nine.'
The proof for ten intellects is that there are nine spheres, each requiring an intellect, plus the first intellect with no sphere.

Emanation of Ten Intellects: The Two-Aspect Principle
Author explains the emanative process from the First Intellect to the Active Intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the First Intellect produces multiplicity while preserving the principle 'from the One only one proceeds' by identifying its two aspects.

Core Idea
Multiplicity enters the cosmos from the first effect's own essence, not from the One.

This passage presents the emanative cosmology of the Peripatetic philosophers (falasifa). It begins with the metaphysical principle that from the absolutely simple One only one thing can directly emanate. Yet we observe a world of multiplicity. The solution lies in the nature of the first created intellect: though simple in relation to God, it contains within itself two distinct aspects that allow it to be the source of a chain of intellects and celestial spheres. Understanding this two-aspect structure is essential for grasping how Islamic philosophers reconciled divine simplicity with cosmic diversity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Locate the phrase 'al-wāḥid lā yaṣdur ʿanhu illā al-wāḥid' (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد). This is the axiom that the One (God) can only produce one immediate effect. All subsequent multiplicity must arise from that first effect, not from God directly.
This principle is foundational in Avicennan metaphysics.
2
Find the two aspects of the first intellect
The first intellect, when considered in itself, has two aspects: wājib bi-l-ghayr (necessity by another) and mumkin li-dhātih (possibility in itself). These arise because the intellect is caused by God (so its existence is necessitated by its cause) but its own essence is contingent.
These two aspects are distinct and allow the intellect to produce two different effects.
3
Map each aspect to its effect
The nobler aspect (wājib bi-l-ghayr) is the source of the next intellect (a more perfect effect). The other aspect (mumkin li-dhātih) is the source of the outermost celestial sphere. The author states that the nobler effect follows the nobler cause.
This pairing is consistent: from every intellect, the necessity aspect produces another intellect, and the possibility aspect produces a sphere.
4
Extend the pattern through the chain
Each subsequent intellect also has the same two aspects, so it too produces another intellect and a sphere. This continues until the ninth intellect, which produces the lunar sphere and a tenth intellect (the Active Intellect).
The first intellect has no sphere because it is the direct emanation from God; its effect is only two, but the first effect itself is an intellect without a sphere.
5
Verify the counts
The author concludes that there are ten intellects and nine spheres. The first intellect has no corresponding sphere, while intellects 2-10 each have a sphere (totalling nine spheres). This matches the observable number of celestial spheres in Ptolemaic astronomy.
The Active Intellect (tenth) does not have a sphere because it governs the sublunary world.
