⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في كيفية توسط كثيرة؛ لما بينا أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، والعقل الذي يصدر عنه الفلك الأعظم فيه كثرة، لكن لا باعتبار صدوره عن واجب الوجود، بل باعتبار أن له ماهية ممكنة الوجود لذاتها واجبة الوجود لعلتها، فيلزمه وجوب الوجود بالغير وإمكان الوجود لذاته، فيكون بأحد هذين الاعتبارين مبدأ للعقل الثاني، وبالاعتبار الآخر مبدأ للفلك الأعظم والمعلول الأشرف يجب أن يكون تابعا للجهة التي هي أشرف الجهات في العقل، فيكون بما هو موجود واجب الوجود بالغير مبدأ للعقل الثاني، وبما هو موجود ممكن الوجود لذاته مبدأ للفلك الأعظم، وبهذا الطريق يصدر عن كل عقل عقل وفلك، وكذلك إلى العقل الأول الذي يصدر عنه الفلك الأعظم إذا لوحظ إلى ذاته مع قطع النظر عن صدوره عن واجب الوجود يوجد في ذاته كثرة، وباعتبار تلك الكثرة صدر عنه معلولان: الفلك الأعظم والعقل الثاني، وإذا نسب ذلك العقل الأول إلى الواجب الوجود من حيث إنه صادر عنه ومعلول له فهو واحد لا كثير، وإلا لامتنع صدوره عنه؛ لأنه تعالى بسيط محض واحد، وقد تقرر عندهم أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد. الطريق الذي يعتبر في كل عقل جهتان: جهة الوجوب بالغير وإمكان الوجود لذاته، والمعلول الأشرف يكون تابعا للجهة التي هي أشرف، فيصدر عن كل عقل بما هو واجب بالغير عقل، وبما هو ممكن لذاته فلك، وكذا إلى أن ينتهي إلى العقل التاسع، فيصدر عنه فلك القمر الذي يلي كرة النار، وعقل عاشر، ولعلهم إنما اكتفوا على العاشر؛ لأن الدليل على وجود العقول كثرة الأفلاك، والأفلاك تسعة، والصادر الأول عن الباري تعالى عقل محض ليس بإزائه فلك، ثم يصدر عن العقل الأول عقل وفلك، وهكذا إلى العقل التاسع، فصار عدد العقول عشرة، وعدد الأفلاك تسعة.
Chapter on the manner of many intermediations: Since we have explained that the One only produces one, and the intellect from which the outermost sphere originates contains multiplicity, but not by virtue of its origination from the Necessary Existence, rather by virtue of its having an essence possible in itself and necessary by its cause, then it entails necessity by another and possibility in itself. Thus, by one of these two aspects it is a principle for the second intellect, and by the other aspect it is a principle for the outermost sphere. And the nobler effect must follow the nobler of the aspects in the intellect. So, by being a necessary existent by another it is a principle for the second intellect, and by being a possible existent in itself it is a principle for the outermost sphere. In this way, from each intellect emanates an intellect and a sphere, and likewise to the first intellect from which the outermost sphere originates: when it is observed in itself, disregarding its origination from the Necessary Existence, there is multiplicity in its essence, and by virtue of that multiplicity two effects emanated from it: the outermost sphere and the second intellect. When that first intellect is related to the Necessary Existence insofar as it is an emanation and effect of Him, it is one, not many; otherwise its emanation would be impossible, because He, exalted, is simple, pure one, and it has been established for them that the One only produces one. The method is that in every intellect two aspects are considered: the aspect of necessity by another and possibility in itself, and the nobler effect follows the nobler aspect. Thus, from every intellect, insofar as it is necessary by another, emanates an intellect, and insofar as it is possible in itself, emanates a sphere. This continues until it reaches the ninth intellect, from which emanates the sphere of the moon that adjoins the sphere of fire, and a tenth intellect. Perhaps they limited it to ten because the evidence for the existence of intellects is the multiplicity of spheres, and the spheres are nine. The first emanation from the Exalted Creator is a pure intellect with no sphere opposite it. Then from the first intellect emanates an intellect and a sphere, and so on to the ninth intellect. Thus, the number of intellects becomes ten and the number of spheres nine.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage defines the emanationist cosmology of the Peripatetic philosophers. It begins by restating the principle that from the One (God) only one thing can directly emanate, because He is absolutely simple. The first emanation is a pure intellect. However, this first intellect, when considered in itself, has a twofold aspect: it is necessary by another (its cause) and possible in itself (its own essence). Because it has these two aspects, it produces two effects: from the aspect of necessity by another comes a second intellect, and from the aspect of possibility in itself comes the outermost celestial sphere. The author notes that the nobler effect (the intellect) must follow the nobler aspect. This pattern continues: each intellect, having the same two aspects, produces another intellect and a sphere. The process ends with the ninth intellect, which produces the lunar sphere and a tenth intellect (the Active Intellect). The author justifies the number of intellects (ten) by noting there are nine spheres, and the first emanation has no sphere—thus nine spheres correspond to intellects two through ten, totaling ten intellects. The argument is that multiplicity in the cosmos is introduced not from God but from the first effect's own essence.

Key Terms
العقل
الواجب بالغير
الممكن لذاته
الفلك
العقل الفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because the First Intellect has two aspects: necessity by another and possibility in itself.
C) Because the sphere is nobler than the intellect.
D) Because the First Intellect is simple and produces many.

Key Takeaway
The first intellect's internal multiplicity (necessity by another and possibility in itself) is the key to explaining how a single first effect can produce a chain of intellects and spheres.

The Soul's Incorruptibility: Refuting the First Possibility
Author rejects the first possibility of the soul's corruption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's trilemma for the soul after death and identify the logical grounds for rejecting the possibility of corruption.

Core Idea
The soul cannot die because corruption requires a substrate that perishes while something else remains.

The author presents three possibilities for the soul after the body's destruction. The first—that the soul itself becomes corrupted—is dismissed with a logical argument. Let's walk through the trilemma and see how the argument works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
List the three options the author presents for the soul after death: corruption (فساد), transfer to another body (تناسخ), or remaining unattached (بقاء بلا تعلق).
2
Locate the rejection of the first possibility
Find where the author says 'لا سبيل إلى الأول' (the first is not possible) and the reason that follows.
The reason begins with 'إذ النفس لا تقبل الفساد'.
3
Analyse the conditional argument
The author argues: if the soul admitted corruption, it would contain a part that admits corruption and a part that is actually corrupted. This is impossible because the soul is simple.
The key line: 'وإلا لكان فيها شيء يقبل الفساد وشيء يفسد بالفعل'.
4
Understand the implied premise
The argument assumes the soul is simple and indivisible. If it had parts, one part would remain while the other perishes, contradicting the nature of the soul.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أحوال النشأة الأخرى:
خاتمة
هداية (۱) النفس بعد خراب البدن إما أن تفسد أو تتعلق ببدن آخر على سبيل التناسخ، أو تبقى موجودة بلا تعلق، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ إذ النفس لا تقبل الفساد، وإلا لكان فيها شيء يقبل الفساد وشيء يفسد بالفعل؛ لأن الفاسد بالفعل غير القابل للفساد، فتكون هداية (۱) سمي أحوال الدار الآخرة للنفس هدايات؛ لدفع أوهام المنكرين لها. التناسخ: أي الانتقال من بدن إلى بدن آخر غير القابل لأن الفاسد ينعدم والقابل يجب بقاؤه مع المقبول، قيل عليه ليس معنى قبول الشيء للعدم والفساد أن ذلك الشيء يبقى متحققا ويحل فيه الفساد على قياس قبول الجسم للأعراض الحالة فيه، بل معناه أن ذلك الشيء ينعدم في الخارج، وإذا حصل ذلك الشي في العقل وتصور العقل معه العدم الخارجي كان العدم الخارجي قائما به في العقل على معنى أنه متصف به في حد نفسه في العقل لا في الخارج؛ إذ ليس في الخارج شيء وقبول عدم قائم بذلك الشيء.
On the states of the other creation:
Conclusion
Guidance (1): The soul after the destruction of the body either becomes corrupted, or attaches to another body by way of reincarnation, or remains existing without attachment. The first is not possible, because the soul does not admit corruption; otherwise there would be in it something that admits corruption and something that is actually corrupted, because the actually corrupted thing is not that which admits corruption. Thus the Guidance (1): The states of the afterlife for the soul are called 'guidances' to repel the illusions of those who deny them. At-tanasukh: meaning the transfer from one body to another. That which does not admit [corruption] because the corrupted perishes and that which admits must remain along with that which is admitted. It was objected to this: The meaning of a thing's admitting non-existence and corruption is not that the thing remains actual and corruption inheres in it analogously to a body's receiving accidents inhering in it, but rather its meaning is that the thing becomes non-existent in the external world. When that thing occurs in the intellect and the intellect conceives with it external non-existence, then external non-existence is subsisting in it in the intellect, in the sense that it is described by it in itself in the intellect, not in the external world, because there is nothing in the external world and an admitting of non-existence subsisting by that thing.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a short concluding section on the states of the soul after death. It presents three possibilities: the soul is corrupted, it transmigrates to another body, or it remains unattached. The author quickly rejects corruption on the grounds that the soul does not admit of corruption; if it did, it would have a part that corrupts and a part that does not, which is absurd. Thus the soul is incorruptible. The author then clarifies that 'reincarnation' (tanasukh) means transfer from one body to another, and that the soul cannot be corrupted because the corrupted thing perishes while the substrate (the soul) that admits corruption must persist—a contradiction. A further subtle point is made about the meaning of 'admitting non-existence': it does not mean that non-existence subsists in the thing like an accident, but that the thing itself ceases to exist in the external world, while in the intellect the concept of non-existence can be predicated of it. The passage defends the survival of the soul and rejects reincarnation, leaning toward the third option: the soul remains without attachment, consistent with Islamic eschatology.

Key Terms
الفساد
التناسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Corruption, annihilation, and paradise
B) Corruption, reincarnation, and remaining without attachment
C) Reincarnation, sleep, and union with the divine
D) Annihilation, transmigration, and resurrection

Key Takeaway
The soul does not admit corruption; therefore, its fate must be either reincarnation or remaining unattached.

Defining Reincarnation (al-Tanasukh)
Author defines tanasukh and clarifies the concept of 'admitting corruption'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define tanasukh as the transfer of the soul from one body to another and explain why it is rejected in this context.

Core Idea
Reincarnation is the transfer of the soul to another body, but it is rejected because it implies the soul's corruptibility.

After rejecting corruption, the author defines the second possibility: tanasukh. He then presents an objection that clarifies the meaning of 'admitting non-existence'. Let's break down the definition and the debate.

Guided Path
1
Find the definition of tanasukh
Locate the phrase 'التناسخ: أي الانتقال من بدن إلى بدن آخر' and note that it is a concise definition.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says that 'admitting non-existence' does not mean the thing remains while non-existence inheres in it; rather, the thing ceases to exist in the external world.
The objection begins with 'قيل عليه ليس معنى قبول الشيء للعدم والفساد...'
3
Analyse the response
The author explains that non-existence is only in the intellect, not in reality. The thing itself does not 'exist' while admitting non-existence.
The response shows that 'admitting corruption' is not a property of an existing thing.
4
Evaluate the rejection of reincarnation
If the soul were to transmigrate, it would have to be capable of existing in multiple bodies, which would imply corruptibility or change. The author's clarification supports the soul's permanence without attachment.

The Meaning of 'Admitting Non-Existence'
Author clarifies the logical meaning of 'admitting non-existence'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between two interpretations of 'admitting non-existence' and identify which one is correct for the soul.

Core Idea
For a thing to admit non-existence does not mean non-existence inheres in it; it means the thing itself ceases to exist.

The author's rejection of corruption rests on a subtle point: what does it mean for something to 'admit corruption'? An objector raises a clarification that refines the concept. This lesson unpacks that philosophical refinement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says the meaning of a thing's admitting non-existence is not that the thing remains and non-existence inheres in it like an accident.
Key phrase: 'ليس معنى قبول الشيء للعدم والفساد أن ذلك الشيء يبقى متحققا ويحل فيه الفساد'
2
Understand the two interpretations
Interpretation 1: Non-existence inheres in an existing thing (like an accident). Interpretation 2: The thing itself ceases to exist in the external world, and non-existence is only in the intellect.
3
Analyse the consequence
Under interpretation 2, the soul cannot be said to 'admit non-existence' as a real capacity, because that would imply a substrate that remains while non-existence occurs.
The author uses this to reinforce that the soul does not admit corruption.
4
Explain the author's position
The author agrees with the objector's refinement, showing that the soul is not something that 'admits' non-existence in the sense of having a potential for annihilation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Three Possibilities for the Soul After Death: Refuting Corruption and Reincarnation
Author lists and refutes the first two possibilities for the soul after body destruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the disjunctive argument for the soul's survival and identify the logical flaws in the refuted possibilities.

Core Idea
The soul survives death because perishing and reincarnation are logically impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a philosophical proof for the soul's immortality. He lays out three exhaustive possibilities and eliminates two through precise reasoning. By following his argument, you will see how disjunctive syllogism combined with conditional reasoning can establish a doctrine of the afterlife without relying on revelation alone.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Find the three options listed: corruption, reincarnation, or remaining without attachment. This is a disjunctive premise covering all logical possibilities.
The disjunction is exhaustive: the soul must be in one of these states after body destruction.
2
Analyze the refutation of corruption
The author argues that if the soul were corruptible, it would be composite (having a part that corrupts and a part that receives corruption). Since the soul is known to be simple, corruption is impossible. This is a conditional proof (modus tollens).
The simplicity of the soul is taken as a given from earlier discussions.
3
Analyze the refutation of reincarnation
Two arguments: (a) souls are created with their bodies, so cannot pre-exist to migrate; (b) each body is sufficient for its own soul's emanation, so two souls on one body is impossible. This eliminates the second option.
The second argument relies on the principle that each body can only receive one soul, which is based on self-perception.
4
Conclude the surviving option
Since the first two are impossible, the third must be true: the soul remains after death without attachment to any body. This is a valid disjunctive syllogism (ponendo tollens).
The conclusion is presented as established (ظهر).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في أحوال النشأة الأخرى: خاتمة هداية (۱) النفس بعد خراب البدن إما أن تفسد أو تتعلق ببدن آخر على سبيل التناسخ، أو تبقى موجودة بلا تعلق، لا سبيل إلى الأول؛ إذ النفس لا تقبل الفساد، وإلا لكان فيها شيء يقبل الفساد وشيء يفسد بالفعل ؛ لأن الفاسد بالفعل غير القابل للفساد، فتكون مركبة، هذا خلف ولا سبيل إلى الثاني؛ لأن النفوس حادثة مع حدوث الأبدان فيكون التناسخ محالا، ولأن البدن الصالح للنفس كاف في فيضان النفس عن مبدئها، فكل بدن يصلح أن يتعلق به نفس، فلو تعلق به نفس أخرى على سبيل التناسخ تعلق بالبدن الواحد نفسان مدبرتان له، وهو محال؛ إذ لا يشعر كل واحد من العقلاء من ذاته إلا نفسا واحدة، فظهر القول ببقاء النفس بعد الموت بلا تعلق.
On the states of the other creation: A conclusion. Guidance (1) of the soul after the destruction of the body: either it becomes corrupt, or it attaches to another body by way of reincarnation, or it remains existing without attachment. The first is impossible, because the soul does not admit corruption; otherwise it would have something that admits corruption and something that actually corrupts, because that which actually corrupts is not that which admits corruption, so it would be composite—this is a contradiction. The second is also impossible, because souls come into existence with the bodies, so reincarnation is impossible, and because a body suitable for a soul is sufficient for the emanation of the soul from its source. So every body that is suitable for one soul, if another soul were to attach to it by reincarnation, two souls would be attached to one body and both would manage it—which is impossible, since every rational person perceives of himself only one soul. Thus the view that the soul remains after death without attachment becomes evident.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the afterlife of the soul. The author enumerates three exhaustive possibilities for the soul after the body's destruction: (1) it perishes, (2) it transmigrates to another body (reincarnation), or (3) it continues to exist without any bodily attachment. The first is refuted by showing that the soul is simple and incorruptible; if it were corruptible, it would have a part that receives corruption and a part that actively corrupts, making it composite, which contradicts the known simplicity of the soul (a standard philosophical axiom). The second is refuted by two arguments: (a) souls are created with their bodies, so a soul cannot exist before its body to migrate to another; (b) each body that can receive a soul is sufficient for the emanation of its own soul from the divine source. If another soul were to attach to it, there would be two souls managing one body, which is impossible because each rational person experiences only one soul. Therefore, only the third possibility remains: the soul survives death without attachment. This conclusion is presented as established (ظهر). The argument uses categorical reasoning with disjunctive premises.

فساد
فيضان

A) Because the soul is a body and bodies cannot corrupt.
B) Because if the soul were corruptible, it would have parts, making it composite, which contradicts its simplicity.
C) Because the soul is created from nothing and cannot be destroyed.
D) Because the soul is an accident that requires a body to subsist.

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive argument that eliminates two possibilities establishes the third by logical necessity, not empirical observation.

Using the same disjunctive form, construct an argument to show that a specific action (e.g., drinking nabidh) must be forbidden if it is either an intoxicant or harmful, and both lead to prohibition.

Refuting Reincarnation: The Irresistible Attachment Argument
Author proves reincarnation invalid by analyzing the nature of soul-body attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the conditional argument against reincarnation and identify why the specific disposition condition defeats transmigration.

Core Idea
If the soul-body attachment is irresistible, it is tied to a specific disposition that perishes with the body.

The author presents a novel argument against reincarnation (tanāsukh). He argues that the soul's attachment to the body is not voluntary but irresistible. From this, he deduces that the attachment must be based on a specific disposition unique to each body, which cannot transfer to another body after death. This argument is self-contained and uses rigorous logical reasoning. In this lesson, we will break down the steps of his proof.

Guided Path
1
Classify the attachment type
The argument begins by presenting a dichotomy: the soul's attachment to the body is either voluntary (ikhtiyārī) or irresistible (iḍṭirārī). Since death is clearly not chosen, the voluntary option is eliminated, leaving irresistible as established.
This is a disjunctive syllogism: either A or B; not B; therefore A.
2
Identify the conditional structure
Once attachment is irresistible, the author asks: what is the criterion (manāṭ) for this attachment? He presents another disjunction: either a specific disposition (isti‘dād makhṣūṣ) for a specific body, or a generic disposition (isti‘dād naw‘ī) for any body of a type. This is framed as a conditional: if irresistible, then criterion is specific or generic.
The antecedent is 'attachment is irresistible'; the consequent is a disjunction.
3
Refute the generic option
The generic disposition is ruled out because it would imply one soul could attach to all bodies of that type simultaneously. This is impossible: a single soul cannot manage two bodies at once, let alone all. Moreover, if it did, it would be a universal intellect, not a soul. Thus, the consequent reduces to the specific option.
This uses a reductio ad absurdum: assume generic, derive contradiction, so reject generic.
4
Conclude the refutation
With the specific disposition established, it follows that this disposition is an accident ('araḍ) that inheres in the body. When the body perishes, its disposition perishes with it. Since the soul cannot attach to a new body without a corresponding disposition, reincarnation is impossible.
The argument relies on the metaphysical principle that accidents cannot transfer to a new substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التناسخ ويمكن أن يستدل على بطلان التناسخ بأن تعلق النفس بالبدن إما أن يكون اختياريا أو اضطراريا، والأول باطل بالضرورة؛ إذ كل عاقل يعلم أن الموت اضطراري ليس باختيار أحد كيف؟ والإنسان يود الحياة والموت يدركه فجاءة، فتعين الثاني فنقول: إذا كان تعلق النفس بالبدن اضطراريا فمناط تعلقها بالبدن إما استعداد مخصوص لبدن مخصوص أو استعداد نوعي لبدن نوعي – والمراد بالاستعداد ههنا حالة للبدن يصح تعلق النفس بها والثاني باطل، وإلا يلزم تعلق النفس الواحدة يجميع الأبدان، واللازم باطل؛ ضرورة أن النفس الواحدة لا تتعلق في زمان واحد ببدنين فضلا عن جميع الأبدان، ولو كانت النفس الواحدة متعلقة بجميع الأبدان لنوع واحد كانت مدبرة لنوع البدن، فلا تكون نفسا، بل عقلا مدبرا لنوع وهو رب النوع عند الإشراقين، فتعين الأول، وهو يستلزم بطلان التناسخ؛ لأن الاستعداد الجزئي المخصوص قد بطل بفساد البدن المخصوص؛ لأن الاستعداد المخصوص وصف للبدن ،المخصوص وبانعدام المحل المخصوص ينعدم الحال المخصوص؛ لامتناع الانتقال على الأعراض عن محالها بالضرورة وهذا مماسنح لي ولم أر في شيء من الكتب، وهذا أقوى الدلائل على بطلان التناسخ، وما ذكره القوم كله أوهن لا يخلو من الجروح، كما في الشروح.
Reincarnation: It is possible to argue for the invalidity of reincarnation by [saying that] the attachment of the soul to the body is either voluntary or irresistible. The first is necessarily false, since every rational person knows that death is irresistible, not by anyone's choice—indeed, a person loves life but death overtakes him suddenly. So the second is established. Then we say: If the soul's attachment to the body is irresistible, then the criterion of its attachment to the body is either a specific disposition for a specific body or a generic disposition for a generic body. By 'disposition' here is meant a condition of the body that makes the soul's attachment to it valid. The second is false; otherwise it would necessitate the attachment of one soul to all bodies. The necessary consequence is false, since one soul does not attach to two bodies at one time, let alone all bodies. And if one soul were attached to all bodies of a single type, it would be the manager of that body type, not a soul, but an intellect managing a type—which is the 'Lord of the Species' according to the Illuminationists. Thus the first is established, which entails the invalidity of reincarnation because the specific particular disposition has been nullified by the corruption of the specific body, since the specific disposition is an attribute of the specific body, and with the non-existence of the specific substrate, the specific attribute ceases to exist, due to the impossibility of the transfer of accidents from their substrates necessarily. And this occurred to me, and I have not seen it in any of the books. This is the strongest evidence for the invalidity of reincarnation. What the others have mentioned is all weaker and not free from wounds, as is [stated] in the commentaries.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a novel and powerful argument against reincarnation (tanāsukh) that the author claims to have originated. The argument proceeds by first classifying the soul's attachment to the body as either voluntary or irresistible. Clearly death is irresistible, so attachment must be irresistible. Given that, the criterion (manāṭ) for attachment is either a specific disposition (isti‘dād makhṣūṣ) tailored to a particular body, or a generic disposition (isti‘dād naw‘ī) that fits any body of a type. The generic option is refuted: if one soul could attach to any body of a type, it would have to attach to all such bodies simultaneously, which is impossible because one soul cannot manage two bodies at once. Moreover, if it managed all bodies of a type, it would be a universal intellect, not a soul. Therefore, the criterion must be a specific disposition unique to each body. Since this disposition is an accident that perishes with the body (its substrate), when the body is destroyed, the disposition disappears, and the soul cannot re-attach to another body. This completes the refutation: reincarnation requires a soul to acquire a new body, but the disposition for that new body would have to arise from that body itself, and the original soul's specific disposition is gone. The author claims this as his original contribution and considers it the strongest proof.

استعداد
مناط

C) Generic dispositions do not exist in nature.
D) The body is one, so the disposition must be specific.

Key Takeaway
The soul's attachment is based on a specific disposition that perishes with its body, making reincarnation impossible.

Defining Pleasure and the Soul's Supreme Delight
Definition of pleasure and its application to the rational soul's highest delight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define pleasure (al-ladhdha) philosophically and identify the highest pleasure of the rational soul according to the commentator of Hidayat al-Hikma.

Core Idea
Pleasure is not mere sensation; it is the perception of the agreeable in its aspect of agreeableness.

This passage from the commentary on Hidayat al-Hikma provides a precise philosophical definition of pleasure and then applies it to the rational soul. Understanding this definition helps distinguish between sensory pleasures and intellectual delights, culminating in the soul's ultimate pleasure: contemplation of the Necessary Being. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure of the definition and its far-reaching implications.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition
Locate the sentence: 'اللذة إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم'. This is the definition. Note that it is not just any perception of what is agreeable, but specifically perceived as agreeable. This 'as such' (من حيث) is critical.
2
Understand the 'as such' restriction
The commentator explains that something agreeable in itself (e.g., a truth) may not be perceived as agreeable if the perceiver is distracted. Hence, pleasure requires both the object being agreeable and the perceiver apprehending it under that aspect.
He gives the example of people engrossed in worldly pleasures who miss intellectual truths.
3
Apply the definition to different faculties
Each faculty (taste, sight, etc.) has its own 'agreeable' (e.g., sweet for taste, light for sight). The definition holds universally: pleasure arises when a faculty perceives its proper object under the aspect of agreeableness.
This shows the definition is not limited to the intellect but applies to all perception.
4
Identify the rational soul's agreeable object
The rational soul's agreeable is the perception of intelligibles, and the noblest of these is the First Truth (al-Ḥaqq al-Awwal) – the Necessary Existence. The soul's pleasure is greater when the object is more noble and subtle.
The passage lists attributes of the First Truth: Necessary Existence, perfect, source of good.
5
Recognize the ultimate pleasure and its consequence
Contemplation of the First Truth and what follows from Him leads to an imprinted image of all existence in the soul, causing intense joy that may result in ecstatic utterances like 'Anā l-Ḥaqq'. The passage cautions that such utterances are excused only in states of spiritual intoxication.
This bridges philosophy and Sufism, showing the pinnacle of intellectual pleasure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هداية (٢) اللذة إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم كالحلو عند الذوق، والنور عند البصر، والملائم للنفس الناطقة إنما هو إدراك المعقولات بأن تتمكن من تصور قدر ما يمكن أن يتبين من الحق الأول، وأنه واجب الوجود لذاته في جميع جهاته بريء عن النقائص، منبع لفيضان الخير على الوجه الأصوب، ثم إدراك ما يترتب عليه بعده. معنى: إنما قيده بالحيثية؛ لأن الشيء قد يكون ملائما في نفس الأمر، ولكن لم تدركه النفس الناطقة من حيث هو ملائم فلا تلتذ به كما ترى الناس لا يلتذون بإدراك الحقائق بانهماكهم في اللذات الدنيوية وتوهم قوم أن اللذة في المطاعم والمناكح فأورد لدفع هذا التوهم هداية، وبين أن اللذة هو إدراك الملائم من حيث هو ملائم ولكل مدرك فيما يدرك إن كان ذلك ملائما لذة، فلذة الذائقة في المذوقات الطيبة ولذة البصر في الصور والأشكال الحسنة، وهكذا لكل حس، النفس الناطقة في المعقولات الحقة، وكل معلوم كان أشرف وألطف كانت لذتها به أكمل وأعظم وأشرف المعقولات وألطفها الحق الأول الواجب الوجود المستجمع لجميع صفات الكمال المقدس عن جميع النقائص والزوال، فكلما لاحت على لوح النفس صورة من هذه الصور، ولمع نور من أنوار الصفات، التذت بها لذة عظيمة تكاد تخرج من البدن من شدة الفرح، ولذا ترى العارفين الغائصين في لجج المعرفة إذا برقت على قلوبهم أنوار وتجليات يغشى عليهم وكادوا أن يموتوا. وبالجملة: ملائم النفس إدراك الواجب لذاته وصفاته قدر ما يمكن، ثم إدراك ما يترتب عليه من العقول والنفوس والأجرام السماوية والكائنات العنصرية، حتى تصير بارتسام صور الموجودات الخارجية كلها من نور الحق الأول إلى آخر الموجود، كأنها هي الكل، وربما التبس على العارف الوجود الأصلي والوجود الظلي، فيرى الظلي كأنه الأصلي، فيرى نور الحق عين الحق، فيجري على لسانه في سكره: أنا الحق، وهو معذور بسكره، وإن كان في صحوه فكفر، أعاذنا الله منه.
Guidance (2): Pleasure is the perception of the agreeable inasmuch as it is agreeable, like sweet in taste, and light in sight. The agreeable for the rational soul is only the perception of intelligibles, that it may be able to conceive as much as can be revealed of the First Truth, that He is Necessary Existence in Himself in all His aspects, free from imperfections, the source of the emanation of good in the most correct manner, then the perception of what follows after Him. Meaning: He only restricted it by the aspect (al-ḥaythiyya) because something may be agreeable in itself, but the rational soul does not perceive it as agreeable, so it does not take pleasure in it, as you see people do not take pleasure in perceiving truths due to their immersion in worldly pleasures. Some people imagined that pleasure is in foods and marriages, so Hidayat al-Hikma advanced this to repel that misunderstanding, and explained that pleasure is the perception of the agreeable inasmuch as it is agreeable, and for every perceiver there is a pleasure in what it perceives if that is agreeable. So the pleasure of the taste faculty is in pleasant tastes, the pleasure of sight in beautiful images and forms, and likewise for each sense. The rational soul's pleasure is in true intelligibles, and the more noble and subtle a thing known, the more complete and greater is the pleasure from it. The most noble and subtle of intelligibles is the First Truth, the Necessary Existence, who encompasses all perfect attributes, holy from all imperfections and change. So whenever an image of these images shines on the tablet of the soul, and a light from the lights of His attributes gleams, it takes great pleasure that almost makes it leave the body from the intensity of joy. For this reason you see the knowers (ʿārifīn) diving in the depths of knowledge, when lights and theophanies flash upon their hearts, they faint and almost die. In sum: the agreeable for the soul is the perception of the Necessary in His essence and attributes as much as possible, then the perception of what follows from Him of intellects, souls, celestial bodies, and elemental beings, so that by imprinted images of all external existents from the light of the First Truth to the last existent, it becomes as if it is the whole. And perhaps the knower confuses the original existence and the shadow existence, so he sees the shadow as if it is the original, and sees the light of the Truth as the Truth itself, so he utters in his ecstasy: 'I am the Truth' (Anā l-Ḥaqq). He is excused in his ecstasy, but if in his sober state, it is disbelief. May Allah protect us from that.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical definition of pleasure (al-ladhdha) and an exposition of the highest pleasure of the rational soul. The author begins with the standard definition: pleasure is the perception of the agreeable (al-mulā'im) precisely insofar as it is agreeable, citing sensory examples like sweetness and light. He then restricts this by 'al-ḥaythiyya' (the aspect of agreeableness) to distinguish between what is inherently agreeable but not perceived as such—for instance, a person engrossed in worldly pleasures may not take pleasure in intellectual truths because they do not perceive them as agreeable. This is a gloss on the main text of Hidayat al-Hikma, which the author (the commentator) explains. The text then clarifies that each faculty has its own appropriate pleasure: taste enjoys pleasant tastes, sight enjoys beautiful forms, and the rational soul enjoys true intelligibles. The noblest intelligible is the First Truth—Allah as Necessary Existence, possessing all perfections. The more noble the intelligible, the greater the pleasure. The soul's ultimate pleasure is in contemplating the Necessary Being and then all that flows from Him (intellects, souls, celestial bodies, elemental beings). Through such contemplation, the soul becomes like a mirror reflecting the entire cosmos, leading to an ecstatic state where the mystic may utter 'Anā l-Ḥaqq' (I am the Truth), a state of spiritual intoxication for which he is excused, but if uttered in sobriety it would be disbelief. The passage bridges philosophy and Sufism, showing the culmination of intellectual pleasure in theophany.

Key Terms
اللذة
الملائم
الحق الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Pleasure is the perception of the agreeable precisely as agreeable, and the rational soul's highest pleasure lies in contemplating the First Truth, leading to ecstatic union that must be guided by sobriety.

Intellectual Pain: Definition and Post-Mortem Occurrence
Author defines intellectual pain and explains its post-mortem occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define intellectual pain by identifying its genus and differentia, and explain why it occurs after death.

Core Idea
Intellectual pain is the soul's perception of its own imperfection after death, when bodily distractions cease.

In this passage, the author presents a precise definition of intellectual pain and addresses a key question: why does the soul not feel this pain in worldly life? Understanding this definition helps clarify the nature of the soul and its post-mortem experience. We will dissect the definition and trace the logical explanation for its delayed occurrence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and definiens
Find the term being defined (الألم العقلي) and the phrase that defines it (إدراك المنافي حيث هو مناف, with the specific contrary for the rational soul).
The definiens is complex, so pay attention to the qualification 'insofar as it is contrary'.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'perception of the contrary' (إدراك المنافي). The differentia is the specific contrary: 'the state opposite to perfection' (الهيئة المتضادة للكمال) for the rational soul.
The differentia also includes the subject 'rational soul' implicitly.
3
Understand the 'insofar as' qualification
The author adds 'insofar as it is contrary' to exclude cases where something is contrary but not perceived as such due to obstacles like bad character. This refines the definition.
This prevents overgeneralization.
4
Explain why intellectual pain occurs after death
The soul perceives the contrary only when the obstacle of bodily attachments is removed. In this life, sensory pleasures dominate, preventing the soul from attending to its perfection.
The refutation of the doubt shows that the cause of pleasure is present but hindered.
5
Consider the refutation of the doubt
If perception leads to pleasure, why is pleasure not felt in life? The author answers that sensory pleasures occupy the soul, so it does not attain its essential perfection until after death.
This strengthens the definition by addressing a potential objection.

من العقول المجردة والنفوس الفلكية والأجرام السماوية والكائنات العنصرية، حتى تصير النفس بحيث يرتسم فيها جميع صور الموجودات على الترتيب الذي هو لها، وهذا الإدراك حاصل لها بعد الموت أيضا، فتكون اللذة حاصلة بعد الموت. وإنما قلنا: إن هذا الإدراك حصل لها بعد الموت؛ لأن النفس لا تحتاج في تعقلاتها إلى الآلة الجسدانية، فيكون تعقلاتها حاصلة بعد الموت، وعدم حصولها حالة تعلق النفس بالبدن إنما كان لقيام المانع، وهو التعلقات البدنية والعلائق الجسمانية. هداية (۳) : الألم إدراك المنافي من حيث هو مناف، والمنافي للنفس الناطقة إنما هو الهيئة المتضادة للكمال، فالنفس إذا فارقت البدن وتمكنت فيها الهيئة المضادة للكمال، أدركت، فيعرض لها الألم العقلي. لا تحتاج لأن إدراكها للأمور الكلية بواسطة القوة العاقلة لا بواسطة القوى الجسمانية، وإنما تحتاج إلى القوى الجسمانية في إحساس الجزئيات المادية. حصولها: دفع وهم يرد وهو أن الإدراك لو كان لذة لحصلت اللذة ههنا؛ لأن علة اللذة متحققة في الحياة فما بال النفس لا تلتذ؟ فأجاب عن هذا الوهم أن اللذات الحسية في الدنيا غالبة على اللذات العقلية، وللنفس بسبب التعلق بالبدن شغل تام باللذات الجسمانية، فلذلك لم تنته إلى كمالها الذاتي فإذا فارقت البدن وانعدمت اللذات الحسية لفقد آلاتها، رجعت إلى كمالها الذاتي وعلمت كمالها فالتذت به. من حيث إلخ وقيده بالحيثية؛ لأن الشيء قد يكون منافيا في نفس الأمر، ولكن لا يدركه من حيث هو مناف؛ لمانع كالأخلاق الذميمة. الألم العقلي: لأن الألم هو إدراك المنافي، وقد حصل للنفس بعد المفارقة، فتألم ألما شديدا بحيث لا يمكنها تدارك ما فات، ولم تألم في الدنيا لمانع، وهو الشغل باللذات الجسمانية.
From the incorporeal intellects, celestial souls, heavenly bodies, and elemental beings, until the soul becomes such that all forms of existents are imprinted in it in the order proper to them, and this perception occurs for it after death as well, so pleasure occurs after death. We only say that this perception occurs after death because the soul does not need the bodily instrument in its intellections, so its intellections occur after death, and their non-occurrence during the state of the soul's attachment to the body was only due to the existence of an obstacle, namely bodily attachments and physical relations. Guidance (3): Pain is the perception of the contrary insofar as it is contrary, and the contrary for the rational soul is only the state opposite to perfection. So when the soul leaves the body and the state opposite to perfection settles in it, it perceives, and thus intellectual pain occurs to it. It does not need [bodily instruments] because its perception of universals is through the rational faculty, not through bodily powers; it only needs bodily powers for sensing material particulars. Its occurrence: A refutation of a doubt that arises: if perception were pleasure, then pleasure would occur here, because the cause of pleasure is present in life, so why does the soul not take pleasure? He answered this doubt by saying that sensory pleasures in this world dominate intellectual pleasures, and the soul, due to its attachment to the body, is fully occupied with bodily pleasures, so it does not attain its essential perfection. When it leaves the body and sensory pleasures cease due to the loss of their instruments, it returns to its essential perfection, knows its perfection, and thus takes pleasure in it. 'Insofar as' etc.: he restricted it with the qualification 'insofar as' because something may be contrary in actual fact, but one does not perceive it insofar as it is contrary, due to an impediment, like blameworthy character traits. Intellectual pain: because pain is the perception of the contrary, and it occurs for the soul after separation, so it suffers intense pain such that it cannot make up for what was lost, and it did not suffer in this world due to an obstacle, namely occupation with bodily pleasures.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines intellectual pain and explains its occurrence after death, along with a refutation of a potential objection. The author begins by describing how the soul acquires the forms of all existents, and that this perception continues after death, leading to pleasure. He then gives the reason: the soul does not need bodily organs for intellection, so its intellections persist after the body's removal; the impediment during life is bodily attachments. Next, the core definition: pain is the perception of what is contrary, and for the rational soul, the contrary is the state opposite to perfection. Therefore, when the soul leaves the body and acquires that opposite state, it perceives it and experiences intellectual pain. The author then clarifies that the soul perceives universals through the rational faculty, not bodily powers, so it does not need the body for this. He then addresses a doubt: if perception is pleasure, why is pleasure not felt in worldly life? The answer is that sensory pleasures dominate and occupy the soul, preventing it from attaining its perfection and thus from feeling intellectual pleasure. Once the body is removed, the soul returns to its essential perfection and experiences pleasure. The author also explains the qualification 'insofar as' to show that something may be contrary in itself but not perceived as such due to obstacles like bad character. Finally, he concludes that intellectual pain occurs after separation because the soul perceives the contrary, leading to severe pain that cannot be remedied, unlike in this life where bodily preoccupations prevent it.

Key Terms
الألم العقلي
المنافي
الهيئة المتضادة للكمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the rational soul not experience intellectual pain during worldly life?
A) Because the soul is not yet separated from the body and is preoccupied with sensory pleasures.
B) Because intelligence is not fully developed until after death.
C) Because pain only occurs when the soul perceives material things.
D) Because the soul does not have the rational faculty in this life.

Key Takeaway
Intellectual pain is the rational soul's perception of its own imperfection, which occurs after death when sensory distractions are removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition of intellectual pain provided in this passage. Then, check whether the definition qualifies as a complete real definition (hadd haqiqi).
?
Using the same definitional structure, construct a definition for 'intellectual pleasure' (al-ladhdha al-aqliyya) as it can be inferred from this passage. What would be its genus and differentia?
